Foreword
Introduction

I

n the span of approximately one year, beginning in
August 2011, the State of New York experienced
three extreme weather events. Hurricane Irene,
Tropical Storm Lee, and Superstorm Sandy wreaked
havoc on the lives of New Yorkers and their
communities. These tragic disasters signaled that
New Yorkers are living in a new reality defined by
rising sea levels and extreme weather events that will
occur with increased frequency and power. They also
signaled that we need to rebuild our communities in
a way that will mitigate against future risks and build
increased resilience.
To meet these pressing needs, Governor Andrew
M. Cuomo led the charge to develop an innovative,
community-driven planning program on a scale
unprecedented and with resources unparalleled. The
NY Rising Community Reconstruction (NYRCR) Program,
within the Governor’s Office of Storm Recovery (GOSR),
empowers the State’s most impacted communities with
the technical expertise and funding resources needed
to develop thorough and implementable reconstruction
plans to build physically, socially, and economically
resilient and sustainable communities.

Program Overview
The NYRCR Program, announced by Governor Cuomo
in April of 2013, is a more than $700 million planning
and implementation program established to provide
rebuilding and resiliency assistance to communities
severely damaged by Hurricane Irene, Tropical Storm
Lee, and Superstorm Sandy. Drawing on lessons
learned from past recovery efforts, the NYRCR Program
is a unique combination of bottom-up community
participation and State-provided technical expertise.
This powerful combination recognizes not only that
community members are best positioned to assess the
FOREWORD

needs and opportunities of the places where they live
and work, but also that decisions are best made when
they are grounded in rigorous analysis and informed by
the latest innovative solutions.
Launched in the summer of 2013 and completed in
March 2014, Round I of the NYRCR planning process
included 50 NYRCR Planning Areas, comprising 102
storm-impacted localities. In January 2014, Governor
Cuomo announced a second round of the planning
process, serving an additional 22 storm-impacted
localities. Four of these localities were absorbed into
existing Round I NYRCR Planning Areas, bringing the
number of localities participating in Round I up to 106;
the other 18 localities formed 16 new Round II NYRCR
Planning Areas. Between Rounds I and II, there are 66
NYRCR Planning Areas, comprising 124 localities. The
program serves over 2.7 million New Yorkers and covers
nearly 6,500 square miles, which is equivalent to 14%
of the overall State population and 12% of the State’s
overall geography.
In Rounds I and II, the State allotted between $3 million
and $25 million to each participating locality for the
implementation of eligible projects identified in the
NYRCR Plan. The funding for these projects is provided
through the U.S. Department of Housing and Urban
Development (HUD) Community Development Block
Grant—Disaster Recovery (CDBG-DR) program.1
Each NYRCR Planning Area is represented by a NYRCR
Planning Committee composed of local residents,
business owners, and civic leaders. Members of the
Planning Committees were identified in consultation
with established local leaders, community organizations
and, in some cases, municipalities. The NYRCR Program
sets a new standard for community participation in
recovery and resiliency planning, with community
members leading the planning process. Across the
State, more than 650 New Yorkers have represented
their communities by serving on Planning Committees.
Nearly 650 Planning Committee Meetings have been
held, during which Planning Committee members
1 Five of the Round I Planning Areas—Niagara, Herkimer, Oneida, Madison,
and Montgomery Counties—are not funded through the CDBG-DR program.

worked with the State’s team to develop community
reconstruction plans, which identify opportunities
to make their communities more resilient. All
meetings were open to the public. An additional 250+
Public Engagement Events attracted thousands of
community members, who provided feedback on the
planning process and resulting proposals. The NYRCR
Program’s outreach has included communities that
are traditionally underrepresented, such as immigrant
populations and students. All planning materials are
posted on the program’s website (www.stormrecovery.
ny.gov/nyrcr), providing several ways for community
members and the public to submit feedback on the
program and materials in progress.
Throughout the planning process, Planning Committees
were supported by staff from GOSR, planners from
New York State (NYS) Department of State and NYS
Department of Transportation, and consultants
from world-class planning firms that specialize
in engineering, flood mitigation solutions, green
infrastructure, and more.
The NYRCR Program does not end with this NYRCR
Plan. Governor Cuomo has allotted over $700 million
for planning as well as implementing eligible projects
identified in NYRCR Plans. NYRCR Planning Areas are
also eligible for additional funds through the NY Rising
to the Top Competition, which evaluates applications
from Round II NYRCR Planning Committees across three
categories—Regional Approach, Inclusion of Vulnerable
Populations, and Use of Green Infrastructure. The winner of
each category will be allotted a share of the competition’s
$3.5 million to fund additional eligible projects.
In April 2014, Governor Cuomo announced that
projects identified in NYRCR Plans would receive priority
consideration through the State’s Consolidated Funding
Application (CFA) process and charged the Regional
Economic Development Councils (REDCs), which
play an advisory role in the CFA process, to support
NYRCR projects. In December 2014, Governor Cuomo
announced that 24 NYRCR projects received nearly
$12 million in CFA funding. This announcement is an
example of the Governor honoring his commitment to
leverage the work of the NYRCR Planning Committees

to incorporate resilience into other State programs and
to find additional sources of funding for NYRCR projects.
The NYRCR Program is also working with both private
and public institutions to identify existing funding
sources and to create funding opportunities where none
existed before.
The NYRCR Program has successfully coordinated
with State and Federal agencies to help guide the
development of feasible projects. The program has
leveraged the REDC State Agency Review Teams
(SARTs), composed of representatives from dozens
of State agencies and authorities, for feedback on
projects proposed.

The NYRCR Plan
This NYRCR Plan is an important step toward rebuilding
a more resilient community. Each NYRCR Planning
Committee began the planning process by defining the
scope of its planning area, assessing storm damage, and
identifying critical issues. Next, the Planning Committee
inventoried critical assets in the community and
assessed the assets’ exposure to risk. On the basis of
this work, the Planning Committee described recovery
and resiliency needs and identified opportunities.
The Planning Committee then developed a series of
comprehensive reconstruction and resiliency strategies,
and identified projects and implementation actions to
help fulfill those strategies.
The projects and actions set forth in this NYRCR Plan
are divided into three categories. The order in which
the projects and actions are listed in this NYRCR Plan
does not necessarily indicate the Planning Committee’s
prioritization of these projects and actions. Proposed
Projects are projects proposed for funding through
an NYRCR Planning Area’s allotment of CDBG-DR
funding. Featured Projects are projects and actions that
the Planning Committee has identified as important
resiliency recommendations and has analyzed in depth,
but has not proposed for funding through the NYRCR
Program. Additional Resiliency Recommendations
are projects and actions that the Planning Committee
would like to highlight and that are not categorized as
FOREWORD

Proposed Projects or Featured Projects. The Proposed
Projects and Featured Projects found in this NYRCR
Plan were voted for inclusion by voting members of
the Planning Committee. Those voting members with
conflicts of interest recused themselves from voting on
any affected projects, as required by the NYRCR Ethics
Handbook and Code of Conduct.
As part of Round II of the NYRCR Program, the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Planning Area has
been allotted up to $3 million in CDBG-DR funds
for the implementation of eligible projects identified
in this plan.
While developing projects for inclusion in NYRCR
Plans, Planning Committees took into account cost
estimates, cost-benefit analyses, the effectiveness of
each project in reducing risk to populations and critical
assets, feasibility, and community support. Planning
Committees also considered the potential likelihood
that a project or action would be eligible for CDBG-DR
funding. Projects and actions implemented with this
source of Federal funding must satisfy a Federallydesignated eligible activity category, fulfill a national
objective (i.e., meeting an urgent need, removing slums
and blight, or benefiting low- to moderate-income
individuals), and have a tie to the natural disaster
to which the funding is linked. These are among the
factors that GOSR will consider, in consultation with
local municipalities and nonprofit organizations, when
determining which projects and actions are best
positioned for implementation.
The total cost of Proposed Projects in this NYRCR Plan
exceeds the NYRCR Planning Area’s CDBG-DR allotment
to allow for flexibility if some Proposed Projects
cannot be implemented due to environmental review,
HUD eligibility, technical feasibility, or other factors.
Implementation of the projects and actions found in this
NYRCR Plan are subject to applicable Federal, State, and
local laws and regulations, including the Americans with
Disabilities Act. Inclusion of a project or action in this
NYRCR Plan does not guarantee that a particular project
or action will be eligible for CDBG‐DR funding or that it
will be implemented. Projects will be implemented on a
staggered timeline, and the NYRCR Program will choose
FOREWORD

an appropriate State or local partner to implement each
project. GOSR will actively seek to match projects with
additional funding sources, when possible.
In the months and years to follow, many of the projects
and actions outlined in this NYRCR Plan will become a
reality, helping New York not only to rebuild, but also to
build back better.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Executive Summary

Reconstruction Program (NYRCR) Program struck the
right balance between recovery efforts and a resolve for
future resiliency.

Overview

The Town of Blooming Grove, with participation from
the Village of South Blooming Grove, formed a 40.3
square mile planning area focused on identifying flood
mitigation and community resiliency activities under the
purview of the NYRCR Program. The Town of Blooming
Grove NYRCR Plan identifies the most critical needs
and impacts from recent major storms, and highlights
potential solutions to bolster future resiliency, increase
safety and quality of life, and inject incentives for
economic growth.

W

hen visitors arrive in the Town of
Blooming Grove for the first time,
they are stunned by its breathtaking
views and majestic mountains,
charming creeks, and picturesque
valleys. The Town, located in the central/south-central
portion of Orange County and about an hour’s commute
from Manhattan, creates an ideal location for people
seeking the charm of a small town and the convenience
of a nearby large city. The natural vistas that contribute
to the Town’s attractiveness also serve to heighten its
vulnerability to flooding disasters.
In late summer 2011, Hurricane Irene showed exactly
how vulnerable the Town could be. In Orange County, 7
to 15 inches of rain fell from August 24 to 30. In addition
to numerous flooded homes, residential developments
were frequently cut off from the world by flood-swollen
creeks and rivers, as was the case for the Merriewold
Lake neighborhood on either side of Satterly Creek.
Equally as concerning, the access roads to the Mountain
Lodge community remained impassable for several days,
even after the storm ended. Emergency medical and
services personnel could not treat trapped residents,
and feelings of anxiety and fear resounded through the
Town of Blooming Grove (Town). Tropical Storm Lee,
while not as severe as Hurricane Irene, exacerbated
damages and increased stress.
The Town, however, used the storm as a chance to test
its mettle. Like the Town’s original settlers, many of
whom participated in the American Revolutionary War,
local citizens refused to let defeat be the final answer
and committed to developing a long-term strategy to
create a more resilient community. New York Governor
Andrew M. Cuomo’s NY Rising Community

viii

Critical Issues
The Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan process
recognized several critical issues surrounding the Town’s
ability to successfully recover from natural disasters and
to enhance its overall resiliency. The Town of Blooming
Grove NYRCR Planning Committee (Committee) used
a combination of existing plan reviews, technical
analyses, public input, interagency guidance, and
active discussion to identify these issues. As with any
worthwhile challenge, these issues have multiple
facets and are interconnected, requiring complex and
innovative solutions.
The Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan (NYRCR Plan)
summarizes the critical issues and challenges into three
major factors to most effectively address and overcome
these concerns.

The Double-Edged Sword of
Picturesque Landscapes
Many communities with significant historical ties to the
land prosper because of their proximity to waterways,
their charming scenery, and the abundance of local
nature. The Town of Blooming Grove is no exception
to this trend. While agriculture is no longer the
predominate land use, the lush valleys and mountains
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

help to draw new residents to the Town (in addition
to the other draw for residents–the convenience of its
proximity to New York City).
The visual amenities that attract new residents to the
Town, however, increase its risk and susceptibility to
storm damage. The rugged and hilly terrain encourages
fast-moving water, while the local waterways frequently
overtop and flood the restful retreats and charming
homes next to them. Intense storms, such as Hurricane
Irene and Tropical Storm Lee, have inflicted significant
economic hardship on the Town. Some of this hardship
was more apparent, such as the isolated and damaged
homes and businesses. Meanwhile, other damage,
including the impact to agriculture and long-term
economic sustainability, was more difficult to discern.

safe neighborhood accessibility.

The Resilient Mindset: More than
Just Infrastructure Projects
Through the course of the planning process, the Town
of Blooming Grove discovered how frequently a lack
of understanding about flooding and its causes can
contribute to exacerbated conditions and increased
risk. Although many of the projects identified by the
Committee focus on infrastructure and structural
improvements, the Committee recognized one of the
most effective solutions to reduce Town vulnerability
and increase resiliency is to create a resilient mindset.

The Committee is determined to live in
harmony with nature and to seek avenues
that allow the community to enjoy its
greatest natural features, while minimizing
their threat.

Efforts to educate the community about factors that
contribute to flood damage and how they can selfprotect can create positive, long-lasting resiliency.

Impassable Roadways and
Unintended Isolation

Both the Committee and the Town met NYRCR
planning and organizational challenges with the same
indomitable spirit that saw them through the 2011
flooding and storms. The Committee formed a multipronged public engagement process to incorporate
data from as many sources as possible. In addition to
the review of existing plans and studies, the Committee
promoted scientific, detailed cost-benefit analyses and
assessments.

The waterways and bodies of water that serve as the
Town’s most appealing natural amenities are often its
greatest threat. Many neighborhoods were built near
the water and due to geographic considerations, have a
limited number of access routes or have access routes
that are easily flooded.
Recent severe storm events provided a graphic
illustration of the vulnerability of certain neighborhoods
with regards to roadway vulnerability. Lakeshore
Drive, Mountain Lodge Park, and State Route 94 all
experienced unintended isolation from flooded streams
or sheet flow. This prevented emergency personnel
from entering and unprepared residents from seeking
more secure shelter. In some instances, neighborhoods
remained isolated and inaccessible for a few days, even
after the storm ended. The NYRCR Plan recognizes this
concern and developed projects designed to increase
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY

A Community-Driven Process

Most importantly, at every step, the process
deliberately included public participation.
The Committee organized and conducted extensive,
multi-media outreach and Public Engagement Events
to offer residents every opportunity to ensure their
concerns and ideas were addressed. Residents could
choose to participate through attendance at bi-weekly
Committee Meetings, online and hard-copy surveys,
door-to-door visits, radio/social media channels, and
Public Engagement Events.

ix

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

The Public Engagement Events were held at both the
Town Hall and the Senior Center, to take advantage
of local resources and to attract as wide an audience
as possible. The Committee worked to ensure that all
Town residents knew about the opportunities presented
through the NYRCR Program, and that nobody was
excluded from participating by a lack of access to
transportation, lack of access to technology, or because
of a functional or other access need.

The comprehensive communications
strategy deliberately targeted home and
business owners; residents of low to
moderate income, the elderly, and other
vulnerable populations; and organizations
in both the public and private sectors.

Vision Statement
Overarching this effort was a vision adopted by the
Committee early in the planning process.
“To provide a stronger, safer, more resilient
community for the residents of the Town of
Blooming Grove and the Village of South Blooming
Grove by restoring natural systems, expanding the
body of knowledge regarding the causes of local
flood events, creating and implementing projects
to address those causes and current damage, and
planning for flood-safe neighborhoods to reduce
future damage, while maintaining the vitality and
viability of the Town of Blooming Grove.”

A Blueprint For Success
The Blooming Grove Committee chose to promote
projects that focus on long-term resiliency. The NYRCR
Plan seeks to create a sustainable blueprint that
effectively target the communities greatest at-risk assets
and recommends implementable solutions that bolster
critical facilities and essential services.

x

The Committee and the public used a multi-generational
body of knowledge and experience, augmented by
both personal experiences and technical analysis, to
determine the Town’s primary needs, risks, and critical
issues. This culminated in proposed projects to bolster
the Town’s resiliency.
The Committee began by analyzing economic,
healthcare, social services, housing, infrastructure, and
natural and cultural resources to determine prospective
risk and impact. It then developed preliminary strategies
to reduce this risk and to meet the most critical
needs. These needs focus on community health and
safety, resiliency, quality of life, economic growth, and
environmental stewardship.
These strategies became the foundation for projects
and actions proposed in the Town of Blooming Grove
NYRCR Plan.
The Committee chose not to limit itself to structure
and infrastructure projects, which can have a limited
useful life; instead, the planning process explored
projects that fall under a variety of categories:
structure and infrastructure; planning and regulatory
processes; natural resource protection; and education
and outreach.
Projects were organized into three project categories.
This methodology was designed to identify and consider
the full range of potential actions and outcomes,
while providing the Town with clear direction toward
project implementation.
Proposed Projects are projects proposed for
funding through an NYRCR Plan Area’s allotment of
CDBG-DR funding.
Featured Projects are projects and actions that the
Planning Committee has identified as important
resiliency recommendations and has analyzed in
depth, but has not proposed for funding through the
NYRCR Program.

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Additional Resiliency Recommendations are projects
and actions that the Committee would like to highlight
and that are not categorized as Proposed Projects or
Featured Projects.

From Strategies to
Implementable Projects
The Committee identified five strategies in its NYRCR
Plan, around which potential projects and resiliency
actions would center. These strategies highlight the
Town’s overall reconstruction and resiliency needs,
while offering opportunities to address them.
The following strategies resulted in Proposed and
Featured Projects, along with Additional Resiliency
Recommendations. These collectively remediate,
mitigate, rebuild, and promote a resilient Town of
Blooming Grove.

Strategies
STRATEGY 1: Reduce the impact of riverine flooding
on the built environment, including critical facilities,
infrastructure, businesses, and housing.
STRATEGY 2: Improve stormwater management and
drainage systems throughout the Town to decrease risk
for homes, businesses, and residents.
STRATEGY 3: Improve upon existing capabilities for
emergency preparedness, response, sheltering, and
communication.

Projects
The following projects were selected for the
Proposed Project category, due to the multitude of
benefits to the community and project contribution
towards increased resiliency.

Additional Hydrologic (Flood Routing),
Hydraulic, and Stormwater Analysis
This project seeks to conduct a comprehensive study
of all local waterways, lakes, and dams affecting flood
conditions in the NYRCR Plan Area. Additional riverine
and overland (stormwater) hydraulic analyses would
provide information and recommendations. These
would be helpful in the planning and development of
further actions to reduce the extent and severity of
flooding in the Town.

Detailed Inspection Program and
Investment Prioritization Protocol
This project seeks a holistic, sustainable approach to
increase transportation system resiliency toward future
storms, and to address repetitive failures on vulnerable
roadway segments throughout the NYRCR Plan Area.
After fully evaluating the components of the existing
transportation network in the Town, and findings from
the proposed Additional Hydrologic (Flood Routing),
Hydraulic, and Stormwater Analysis Project, the
Committee recommended implementation of a detailed
inspection program and investment protocol for Town‐
owned bridges and culverts.

STRATEGY 4: Preserve, protect, and enhance the Town’s
natural, recreational, and cultural resources, and restore
natural floodplain capabilities.

Install Natural Gas or Solar
Back-up Power for Critical
Facilities and Infrastructure

STRATEGY 5: Assist Town residents, officials, and
business owners in becoming better protected from
future flood damages through education, programming,
outreach, and policy initiatives.

This project aims to obtain and install permanent
back-up power sources for the critical facilities and
infrastructure in the Town that are not addressed in
other Proposed Projects. This project would include

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY

xi

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

an evaluation of existing critical facilities’ needs for
redundant power generation.
The Town could consider the use of solar or green
energy, where feasible, at these locations:
• South Blooming Grove Village Hall; and
• Town Highway Garage at 2741 State Route 94
(also the proposed site for relocation of the Town
of Blooming Grove Central Garage, providing a
secondary benefit to installing a back-up power
source here).

Mountain Lodge Drainage
Improvements and Flood
Damage Reduction
This project would provide a series of standard plans
and detailed drawings to repair or upgrade existing
degraded stormwater ditches. It would then implement
a revolving loan, grant, or cost-share program to
help low-income residents of Mountain Lodge Park
implement improvements on a property-by-property basis.
In addition to providing standard plans, this project
would provide outreach and education to homeowners
through the Homeowners Association to assist in the
implementation of the plans.

• South Blooming Grove Firehouse Stream Bank
Stabilization;
• Merriewold Lake Outlet Channel Bank Restoration;
• Flood mitigation measures for Tappan Waste
Water Treatment Plant (Barnes Road); and
• Flood mitigation measures for Village of South
Blooming Grove.

Update Local Codes and Ordinances
This project would include a comprehensive review of
the zoning and subdivision regulations to identify areas
of improvement that would enhance the Community’s
ability to be more resilient to future storm events and
would address the feasibility related to code changes.

Vulnerable Populations and Community
Emergency Alert, Warning, and Support
Implement and disseminate education materials
regarding a coordinated alerting system and general
disaster readiness. These materials would be designed
for public and private sectors, families and individuals,
and Persons with Access and Functional Needs (PAFN)
and vulnerable populations. The materials can also
benefit low and moderate income populations who may
be without reliable access to Internet service.

Strategic Critical Facility Flood-Proofing
and Structural Mitigation Program
This project aims to strengthen key critical facilities
that experienced service interruptions due to flooding
during Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm Lee. It would
be informed by the Town’s new Detailed Program and
Investment Prioritization Tool. The following projects
are proposed:
• Town Maintenance Garage Relocation;
• Salisbury Mills Fire Department Stormwater
Retrofit;

Section 1:
Community Overview
From Grey Skies to Great
Challenges

W

aterfront properties with amazing
views.” Most folks dream for a home
with this description. Few would
guess, though, that “waterfront
properties with amazing views” also
described many areas in the Town of Blooming Grove,
New York, in late August 2011, before torrents from
Hurricane Irene swelled streets and swallowed cars as
far as the eye could see. The flooding devastation and
resulting debris fields in the aftermath of Hurricane
Irene’s record rainfall grew in magnitude in the wake of
Tropical Storm Lee, just one week later, in early September.
Hurricane Irene was a beastly storm in late summer
2011. Image is courtesy of the National Oceanic and
Atmospheric Administration (NOAA).
The impacts and challenges caused by these storms
and flooding were felt throughout the region. Despite
extensive community collaboration to use available
response and recovery resources, including inspired

local volunteer efforts, considerable unmet recovery
needs remain.
The NYRCR Program empowers communities severely
impacted by Superstorm Sandy, Hurricane Irene, and
Tropical Storm Lee through rebuilding and resiliency
assistance. Under the guidance and funding of the
NYRCR Program, the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan aims to address the most critical needs and impacts
from these storm events, while identifying strategies
and needs for future resiliency, increased quality of life,
community vibrancy, and economic revitalization.
The Village of South Blooming Grove, geographically
connected with the Town of Blooming Grove, is
considered part of the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan Area (NYRCR Plan Area), for the purposes of the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan.

The Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan is a critical complement to other
state, regional, and local emergency
management, economic development, and
community revitalization efforts.

Hurricane Irene was a beastly storm in late summer 2011. Image is courtesy of the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA).
SECTION I: COMMUNITY OVERVIEW

1

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

A Collaborative Approach To
Critical Issues
This Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan is the result
of months of grassroots-level Public Engagement Events
and opinion-gathering that has been corroborated by
hydraulic analysis, additional scientific and quantitative
evaluations, and historical trending. This effort was
steered by the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Planning
Committee (Committee), a collaborative cross-section of
residents, first responders, business people, community
planning specialists, educators, utility industry workers,
and other key stakeholders who met at least biweekly
over a seven-month planning process.
The all-volunteer Committee worked with Tetra Tech,
Inc. (Consultant Team), staff from the New York State
Department of State (NYS DOS), and the NYRCR
Regional Lead. The Consultant Team provided technical
expertise in all areas of planning and recovery, and
supported the Committee in developing materials,
conducting public outreach, performing detailed
analyses, and identifying projects for potential
implementation. Regular Committee Meetings provided
the venue for managing the overall process; assigning
tasks; evaluating and understanding qualitative (public
opinion) and quantitative (scientific) information
gathered; determining the vision and direction of the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan; and ultimately, for
categorizing projects that could be eligible for NYRCR
Program funding.

the Village of South Blooming Grove by restoring
natural systems, expanding the body of knowledge
regarding the causes of local flood events, creating
and implementing projects to address those causes
and current damage, and planning for flood-safe
neighborhoods to reduce future damage, while
maintaining the vitality and viability of the Town of
Blooming Grove.

The Planning Process
The Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan presents the
established community vision, goals, projects, initiatives,
and implementation strategies to further the Town of
Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan Area (NYRCR Plan Area)’s
recovery, and to achieve greater resiliency in the face of
future storm events.
The planning process is implementation-focused and
builds on existing plans and studies to advance recovery
and resiliency steps. The Town of Blooming Grove
NYRCR Plan components, supporting documentation
and analysis, and subsequent recommendations focus
on the six Recovery Support Functions (RSFs) developed
by the Federal Emergency Management Agency (FEMA)
(see Table 1.1).

Recovery Support Function

Focus Area

Community Planning and Capacity Building

The community’s ability to plan and implement storm recovery activities and its ability to
mitigate the effects of future storms.

Economic

The community’s ability to return economic and business activities to healthy states
following a disaster.

Health and Social Services

The community’s ability to identify the key needs critical to protecting the health and wellbeing of all residents (particularly vulnerable populations) from the effects of a post-disaster
environment.

Housing

The community’s ability to provide safe and adequate housing for current and future
residents.

Infrastructure Systems

The community’s ability to replace or improve infrastructure in response to disasters. This
includes roads and bridges, as well as water, sewer, and communications networks.

Natural and Cultural Resources

The community’s ability to leverage natural and cultural resources while protecting manmade assets.

These RSFs comprise the National Disaster Recovery
Framework’s coordinating structure for key functional
areas of assistance in supporting localities. The RSFs
helped facilitate local stakeholder participation and
promoted intergovernmental and public-private
partnerships.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Geographic Scope of the Town
of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan
Historic Snapshot
The Town of Blooming Grove was organized on March
23, 1799, when the territory was taken from Cornwall
Township. The name Blooming Grove had long been
in use for this part of Cornwall to distinguish it from
Hunting Grove, a locality then in New Windsor.
According to local accounts, many of the early settlers
from the area played a key role in the American
Revolution. Numerous roads in the Town bear the
names of Revolutionary War heroes, such as Horton,
Tuthill, Woodull, and Clinton. When the current Town of
Blooming Grove was formed, the old church, erected in
1759, formed its center.

“bedroom community” for workers, visitors, and
second homeowners. It includes a relatively small
commercial area (1.5% of all land uses in the NYRCR
Plan Area), various residential neighborhoods, a sizeable
agricultural area (nearly 18% in the NYRCR Plan Area)
and other land uses.
The NYRCR Plan Area is located in the central and
south-central portion of Orange County, in the Hudson
Valley/Catskills Region. The Town of Blooming Grove is
bordered to the east by the Towns of Woodbury and
Cornwall, to the north by the Towns of New Windsor
and Hamptonburgh, to the west by the Towns of
Chester and Goshen, and to the south by the Town of
Monroe and Village of Kiryas Joel. Figure 1.2 displays
an overview of the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan Area. The Committee considered a review of past
events, sources of historical flooding, and the location
of critical facilities impacted by flooding to determine
NYRCR Plan Area.

Location
The Town of Blooming Grove is one of 20 towns in
Orange County. Orange County is considered one of
the fastest growing counties in the State of New York.
While the Village of Washingtonville and the Village of
South Blooming Grove geographically border the Town
of Blooming Grove, these Villages are self-governing
entities. The Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan Area
comprises 40.3 square land miles and includes the
entirety of the Town of Blooming Grove (35.3 square
miles) and the Village of South Blooming Grove (5.0
square miles), as well as a small area within the
Town of Monroe containing the Orange and Rockland
Lake watershed.
The watershed spans the Blooming Grove and Monroe
Town boundary near the intersection of State Route
17/U.S. Route 6 and County Route 208 (see Figure
1.2). Although the Village of South Blooming Grove is
included in the Plan Area, the Town’s other neighbor,
the Village of Washingtonville is not included as it
participated in Round 1 of the NYRCR planning process.
The Town of Blooming Grove is about a one-hour
commute to Manhattan, making it an attractive
4

The Town of Blooming Grove’s location offers easy access to New
York City, as well as the allure of outdoor recreational opportunities.

Geography
The Town’s uneven topography is characteristic of other
areas in the Hudson Highlands, which have formed over
tens of thousands of years via geologic uprisings, glacial
scouring, and subsequent erosion by wind and water.
Schunemunk Mountain in the east offers ridge-framed
vistas of open fields, lakes and streams, and forested
bottom lands. Town residents easily recognize and
reflect the importance of local landmarks and features,
such as Round Hill, Woodcock Mountain, and Bull Mine
Mountain, in local street and place names.
Rolling pastures are punctuated by surrounding mountains in this
community. Photo is courtesy of Eric Thayer.

Physical Features
The long range of the Schunemunk Mountains form
the eastern boundary of the NYRCR Plan Area, and the
numerous nearby foothills provide a unique historical
element, in that their magnetic iron ore deposits
were used to make weaponry during Civil War times.
Throughout the NYRCR Plan Area, farm fields and open
meadows stretch across the valleys.
Cromeline Creek runs from Walton Lake by the base
of Goose Pond Mountain, through the Greycourt
meadows and the picturesque falls at Craigville, and
until Farmingdale and Hulsetown. The Otterkill Creek
joins the Cromline Creek by the Town of Hamptonburgh
line, and the Tappan or Schunemunk Creek, flowing
from Satterly’s Mills, joins it near the Village of
Washingtonville.
The lush meadows and apple orchards crowning the
hillsides with sweet blossoms justify the name of
Blooming Grove. With a stunning blend of evergreen
and deciduous trees, the autumn view is equally
glorious. This natural majesty of creeks, valleys, and
mountains is quickly replaced by mayhem during
severe storm events, as water that drains from the
mountainside and into the valleys can easily overrun
creek banks and tributaries that crisscross the region.

7

The same topography and natural features that
contribute to the Town’s small-town charm also resulted
in significant flood damage during severe weather
events. The western portion of the Town of Blooming
Grove is hilly, with elevations ranging from 400 to 700
feet above sea level. Meanwhile, the area located just
east and west of Clove Road, extending from the north,
near the Town of Cornwall border, to the south, near the
Town of Monroe border, characterizes itself with many
hills. These hills have elevations ranging from 400 to 850
feet above sea level, and they form a chain that extends
from the northeastern to the southern part of the Town.
In addition to mountains and foothills, the Town’s
diverse landscape includes rolling hills, wooded
hillsides, lakes, streams, rural settlements, suburban
neighborhoods, shops, and retail strips. Town
commercial development has been limited overall, with
the exception of some small strip shopping centers.
About 75% of the Town is undeveloped open space,
with much of it available for residential development.
Residential development continues to increase, similar
to many adjacent communities and consistent with
population growth across all of Orange County.

Nevertheless, the majority of the Town’s
soils can impose severe restrictions to
development due to soil stoniness, steep
slopes, slow permeability, shallow depth to
bedrock, and a seasonal high-water table
that limits land development.

SECTION I: COMMUNITY OVERVIEW

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

These soil considerations are important factors in the
design and density of development. The steep slopes
and bedrock outcroppings typically found in upland
areas, such as Schunemunk Mountain, limit local
construction. Additionally, developers consider the soils
in Town (primarily in the lowland plains and valleys), as
well as the area south of State Route 94 and along the
western border of Town, as generally unsuitable
for development due to poor permeability, ponding,
or flooding.

Waterways
The topography and waterways in the Town make
it vulnerable to damaging wind and floods during
severe storm events, such as those experienced in late
summer 2011. The major waterways in the NYRCR
Plan Area include:
1. Beaverdam Lake

Residential and municipal properties experienced the
primary economic damage, particularly in regards to
structural (i.e., home/residences) and infrastructural
(i.e., Town roads and critical facilities, including fire
stations, the central garage, etc.) repair costs. Local
businesses in and near Station Road Square and
Salisbury Mills also experienced some structural repair
costs, which impacted their ability to operate on a
routine basis. Additional economic damage included
some business and employment interruption, largely
to residents stranded in their neighborhoods, or
those who used public transportation to commute to
New York City.
The first round of the NYRCR Program comprised 102

ny.gov/nyrcr).

Town sustained lasting impacts from the
storms’ devastation.

Hurricane Irene – A Set-up
for Disaster
Out of the three most notable recent disasters, i.e.,
Superstorm Sandy, Hurricane Irene, and Tropical Storm
Lee, Hurricane Irene caused the most damage to the
Town of Blooming Grove. In the weeks before Hurricane
Irene, much of eastern New York State had soil moisture
ranking in the 80th and 90th percentiles. The National
Climactic Data Center (NCDC) determines these
9

Hurricane Irene tracked up the East Coast of the
United States from August 21 to 27, 2011, when wind
speeds decreased to tropical storm forces. Hurricane
Irene made its third landfall in coastal New Jersey and
then hit New York State on August 28, 2011, bringing
heavy rains and flooding to the Catskill Mountains and
throughout the Hudson Valley. Flood waters overtopped
roadway segments in the Town of Blooming Grove,
including Horton Road, Glenwood Road, Tuthill Road,
State Route 208, and State Route 94, limiting the ability
of emergency services personnel to reach residents
for rescue. Bridges on State Route 208, Lake Road,
Merriewold Lane and Tuthill Road became impassable,
diverting residents and commercial traffic miles out of
the way.
SECTION I: COMMUNITY OVERVIEW

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Hurricane Irene, the Satterly Creek rose to wash out or
severely damage each of these roads, leaving residents
without access into or out of their neighborhood.
Nearby in the Village, four homes on Turtle Knoll were
cut off when waters from a private lake on the north
side of the road overtopped the spillway and washed
out the small private bridge accessing this subdivision.
On the other side of Town, near the Village of
Washingtonville, homes on Cardinal Drive and Patricia
Lane, backing up along the Moodna Creek, were so
significantly damaged that many were later offered
FEMA buy-outs.
Pictured above is the Salisbury Mills/Orrs Mill Road power station on
August 28, 2011. The station was overrun with hurricane flood waters.
The volume and velocity of the water rushing over the spillway shown
here was the worst in recent history. Photo is courtesy of Committee
Co-Chair Richard Bachman.

On August 31, 2011, President Barack Obama issued a
major disaster declaration (DR-4020) for New York State
and the counties impacted by Hurricane Irene, including
Orange County and the NYRCR Plan Area.
In Orange County, 7 to 15 inches of rain fell from August
24 to 30, 2011, exacerbating the already-saturated
soil conditions. The County suffered 45 to 50 mile per
hour wind gusts on August 28, 2011. Moodna Creek
overflowed its banks, resulting in water rescues at
Udderly Fresh Farms on State Route 94 near
Farmview Lane.

Access roads in and out of the Town’s
residential subdivisions were severely
damaged, leaving people stranded in their
homes and neighborhoods.
The first floor and basements of numerous homes on
Barnes Road were flooded when a culvert was breached
and water from the Satterly Creek flowed over the road
and into residents’ backyards.
In the Village of South Blooming Grove, Merriewold
Lane, Stonegate Drive, and Lake Shore Drive serve as
the three sole access roads to reach the residential
developments surrounding Merriewold Lake. During
SECTION I: COMMUNITY OVERVIEW

Even after the rains ended, most roads in Mountain Lodge remained
dangerous or impassible. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

The Mountain Lodge community contains about 800
residential units primarily occupied by low to moderate
income households. Mountain Lodge is a former
summer residential community, with most of the access
provided via gravel roads and trails that predate the
current road standards set by the Town of Blooming
Grove. Sheet flow off the mountains damaged many
of the homes’ main accesses and entered the gravel
trails and roads, carrying large debris and gravel in fast
moving waterways. This created extremely dangerous
conditions for residents and forced many to shelter in
place while the flooding continued.

10

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Clove Road, a primary local arterial that connects
Mountain Lodge Park to the rest of the Town, had also
flooded, cutting off access for escaping the dangerous
conditions on the mountain and preventing emergency
services from entering. Two homes in Mountain Lodge
Park burned to the ground in the days following the
storm, before access roads reopened and when fire
trucks could not reach the homes quickly. Another
home in this community was washed away during the
storm, and a few have been abandoned since the storm.

Tropical Storm Lee—
No Rest for the Weary

Salisbury Mills, a primary area of commerce in the Town
of Blooming Grove, also became severely flooded from
stormwaters. Salisbury Mills is located on 94 and Station
Road, nestled between the Moodna Creek and its
tributary flowing from the nearby Beaver Lake.

In the wake of Hurricane Irene and
Tropical Storm Lee, the most common
damages across the NYRCR Plan Area
included flooded roads that forced closures
and cut off ingress and egress to many
homeowners. Embankment failures,
bridge wash-outs, septic tank back-ups,
wastewater runoff, and deluges from
private dams were also noted in damage
reports and were offered anecdotally by
local residents.

This area is home to the Salisbury Fire Department,
a Post Office, and a number of small locally-owned
businesses. Many of these buildings experienced first
floor flooding and had to be rebuilt after the storm. The
Salisbury Fire Department needed almost a complete
rebuild due to the extent of flood damage. Some of the
small businesses in the local shopping plaza did not have
the economic resources to re-open after the flood.
Additionally, State Route 94, Woodcock Mountain Drive,
and Station Road closed briefly as a result of Hurricane
Irene. The hurricane exacerbated historic embankment
failures at the State Route 88 area, between Exits 2 and
3, and the Airport Road Exit embankment failure area,
next to State Route 17.
The Community has experienced a growing local
concern over the impact of uncoordinated dam releases
by private dam owners throughout the Town and
watershed. Without effective emergency operations
agreements between the Town and all local dam
owners, some spillways overtopped with flood waters
from Hurricane Irene, while other dams were released
too late to be effective in providing flood storage to
mitigate downstream flooding. Uncoordinated dam
release, along with suspected reduced stream and lake
capacity due to sediment loading from Hurricane Irene
and other past storms, are some of the most frequently
reported concerns captured by public comment.
11

On September 7, 2011, just a few weeks after Hurricane
Irene and with the ground still saturated, Tropical
Storm Lee added to the accumulation of flooding and
rain damage. Although not as intense as Hurricane
Irene, the storm further destroyed already weakened
infrastructure, limiting the ability of the Town to recover
from the damages incurred from Hurricane Irene.

All of Orange County, including the NYRCR Plan Area,
became eligible for both Public Assistance (PA) and
Individual Assistance (IA) from FEMA, due to Hurricane
Irene. Orange County, including the Town of Blooming
Grove, was only eligible for FEMA PA after Tropical
Storm Lee. The Committee was unable to obtain the
financial amounts for damages suffered by the Town
and assistance provided to the Town of Blooming Grove;
however, Orange County received $29.1 million overall
in assistance between Hurricane Irene and Tropical
Storm Lee (see Figure 1.3).

coordination with the NYRCR Town of
Blooming Grove Plan.

Note: HEC RAS = Hydrologic Engineering Center River Analysis System

Critical Issues
Nature’s Beauty and the Beast
Since its founding in 1799, the Town of Blooming Grove
has grown to offer a blend of suburban neighborhoods,
small villages, rural land vistas, and flourishing
picturesque farms. Its proximity to waterways has
provided ideal fishing spots, restful retreats, and a high
quality of life. Intense storms, such as the one-two
punch experienced with Hurricane Irene and Tropical
Storm Lee, have inflicted significant economic hardship
on tourism and the small amount of local agriculture,
often wiping out crops during both planting and
harvest seasons.

Rural, Rugged, and At-Risk
While the large rural stretches of the Town of Blooming
Grove reflect its allure, impassable roadways to critical
facilities, supplies, and workplaces arise during severe
storm events. Some neighborhoods in the Town are
in remote and rugged terrain. The neighborhood of
Mountain Lodge Park has an enclave of several hundred
homes set on mostly unpaved, deeply-rutted trails on a
steep, tree-shaded side of Schunemunk Mountain. Once
a bustling vacation hub for middle-class families from
New York City, Mountain Lodge Park now contains many
vacant homes and abandoned septic systems.
15

Deep ruts scour the landscape and result in sediment runoff that clogs
culverts and stormwater drainage systems. Photo is courtesy of local
resident, Walter Millet.

Persistent sheet flow issues continuously surface during
storm events in this mountainous area and elsewhere
along Schunemunk Mountain.
Sheet flow is a condition where rainfall, particularly
runoff, distributes itself uniformly over a surface. It
basically resembles a sheet of water on the surface due
to generally being at a very low depth of flow. Sheet
flow very quickly turns to concentrated flow, usually
over a length of 50 to 300 feet. This leads to problems
with exposed surfaces, where it can pick up sediment
and cause pollutant problems. Unpaved surfaces and
nutrient-rich farm fields can also contribute to water
quality issues from the runoff.
SECTION I: COMMUNITY OVERVIEW

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Infrastructure Issues
According to many local residents, the timing of water
release from private lakes and dams in and adjacent
to the Plan Area have exacerbated flood conditions
and deluged stormwater infrastructure during severe
storm events. According to many who attended Town of
Blooming Grove NYRCR Public Engagement Events, this
dynamic contributed to well contamination issues and
sewerage plant pumping station flooding and failures in
the Village of South Blooming Grove.
Some bridges remain at risk to future storms because
their replacement and reconstruction after past events
did not cover an increase in capacity. Furthermore,
undersized and blocked culverts contributed to
additional flooding.
Impassable roadways put people who were stranded
in their homes at increased risk, as the dangerous road
conditions cut them off from medical supplies, food

staples, and emergency services. The increasingly
sizeable elderly population found throughout the
Community brings an additional element of concern
to this risk, as many of the elderly live alone and have
increased medical needs.

According to the Orange County Flood Insurance
Study (FIS) (2009), flooding sources throughout the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan Area include:
• Moodna Creek: From approximately 1,100
feet downstream of the spillway at the Towns
of Blooming Grove and Cornwall until the
confluence with Otter Kill and Cromline Creek.
• Otter Kill: From approximately 1.2 miles
upstream of Sara Wells Trail until approximately
1.2 miles upstream of State Route 17.
• Otter Kill Tributary 12: From its confluence with
Otter Kill until approximately 150 feet upstream
of Craigsville Road.
• Satterly Creek: From its confluence with Moodna
Creek until the confluence with Satterly Creek
Tributary 5.
The next several figures offer graphic depictions of
damages and road closures, risk areas, and flood
hazard areas, respectively.

Relevance of Demographics
During Disaster Events
According to 2012 U.S. Census data, the Town of
Blooming Grove had a total population of 18,028
people, inclusive of the Village of South Blooming Grove
(3,234) and the Village of Washingtonville (5,811). Of
this population, 2,095 (approximately 11.5%) are aged
65 and older, as indicated in Figure 1.7. This aging
population may increasingly require assistance and
specialized care.

An increasingly aging population in the
NYRCR Plan Area, many of whom live alone,
indicates a growing need for evacuation
assistance and guidance for proper
sheltering-in-place during certain severe
weather events.

managers must incorporate more varied language

Transportation routes entirely within the NYRCR Plan

affect the local economy during disaster events. Workers
who cannot get to their place of employment have a
reduced income-earning capacity. This often presents a
secondary economic impact, as it impacts workers’ level
of discretionary spending and leads to less spending at
service-related outlets that have increasingly become
part of the NYRCR Plan Area’s economic drivers.

Because the most prevalent employment sectors in

infrastructure will directly impact the local and

unemployment rate in the Plan Area, as compared to
Orange County and New York State.

Land Use

The NYRCR Plan Area contains a varied mix of
employment sectors, with most residents working in the
educational services, healthcare, and social assistance
sector (24.5%), followed by retail trade (18.4%). A once
light-industrial and agricultural-intensive area, the Town
of Blooming Grove has given way to service and retail-

land uses to generate increased robust local economic
activity, a stronger tax base, and jobs closer to home.
These factors are all significant for people who “outmigrate” for work, particularly when faced with
potential geographic isolation from storm-caused
road closures.
Figure 1.12 indicates that residential/single-family
dwellings consist of about one-third of the land use
in the NYRCR Plan Area, while another third of the
land remains vacant. Depending on ownership, use
restrictions (zoning), and environmental sensitivity
(wetlands, preserves, etc.), vacant land offers a
source of potential future stormwater retention
locations and areas for relocation of critical facilities
out of the floodplain.
While agriculture constitutes just shy of 18% of land use
in the NYRCR Plan Area, severe flooding and subsequent
runoff cause nutrient issues and sediment cast-off in
waterways downstream and other low-lying areas. Large
snow melts, heavy spring rains, and severe summer
storms can delay or ruin planting and harvesting
seasons. This impacts the popular and prevalent
farmers’ markets along local roadways and in the
NYRCR Plan Area. Locals rely on these markets for fresh
food, and the markets serve as an income source for
local farmers.

Improved stormwater management and
increased infrastructure capacity would
help to strike a balance between economic
development and open space.

Source: U.S. Census, 2012

Where People Live
Prior to 1980, more houses served as vacation and
secondary homes in the Plan Area than they do today.
As people moved out of heavily urbanized areas (most
notably, New York City) and into the outlying suburbs
within an hour’s commute to New York City, the
population of Orange County grew, including in the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan Area. The result
has been an influx of higher wage earners who can
afford newer, higher-priced, single-family detached
homes, as indicated in Figure 1.13.
The majority of homes in the NYRCR Plan Area are
owner-occupied and were built after 1939. In fact, the
NYRCR Plan Area consists of a greater percentage of
newer owner-occupied single-family homes than in
Orange County and New York State (see Figure 1.14).
Newer homes are more likely to be built to modern
building standards in compliance with the Town’s
Floodplain Management Ordinance, and may therefore
be better equipped to withstand severe storms.
However, inadequate stormwater infrastructure,
issues associated with dam water releases in the area,
and sewage treatment that has not kept pace with
population growth in the Plan Area remain persistent
problems. These factors, as previously discussed, still
contribute to invading flood waters and septic back-ups.

The NYRCR Plan Area encompasses 86.2
miles of public roads, including 17.3 miles of
state highways, 12.5 miles of county roads,
and 56.4 miles of town roads.
New York State Route 17 (Route 17) is a four-lane
highway running east-west through the southern
portion of the Plan Area. Route 17 provides access to
other major regional highways including: Interstate
84 and Interstate 87 (New York State Thruway). Three
interchanges on Route 17 reside either inside or just
outside of the NYRCR Plan Area: State Route 208 (Exit
130), Museum Village Road (Exit 129), and County
Route 51 (Exit 128). Major roads in the NYRCR Plan
Area include State Route 208 and 94, as well as County
Routes 20 (Orrs Mill Road), 27 (Clove Road), 44 (Seven
Springs Mountain Road/Ridge Road), 51 (Hulsetown/
Craigville Road), and 66 (Craigville Road).
State Route 208 acts as one of the primary access routes
into the NYRCR Plan Area. Collector roads typically
handle less traffic than local highways but serve a similar
function. These roadways tend to be through-streets
and have slower speeds. The collector roads in the Plan
Area include Round Hill Road, Prospect Road, Woodcock
Mountain, Horton Road, Tuthill Road, Helms Hill Road,
and Mountain Lodge Road.

Source: U.S. Census, 2012

Infrastructure
Transportation
ROADS
The NYRCR Plan Area emergency management officials
have identified several transportation corridors as the
major evacuation routes in Town. These include State
Routes 17, 94, and 208, along with County Routes 27
and 44. The NYRCR Plan Area has a convenient location
adjacent to a major regional highway system; however,
only a few of these highways are directly within the
limits of the NYRCR Plan Area.

SECTION I: COMMUNITY OVERVIEW

COMMUTER RAIL AND BUS
The Metro-North Port Jervis Line offers service to
New York City at the Salisbury Mills and Campbell Hall
stations. Bus service provides an alternative form of
public transportation to New York City, with numerous
stops throughout Orange County.

LOCAL TRANSPORTATION
The Town of Warwick provides inter-municipal bus
service. Service routes run from Washingtonville/ and
Blooming Grove, and the Town of Warwick to shopping
centers in the Town of Monroe, the Village of Harriman,
and the Town of Woodbury. Orange County provides
Dial-a-Bus service in the Town of Blooming Grove and
the surrounding area for a small fee. Moreover, the
Blooming Grove Senior Center offers rides to senior
citizens over 60 years of age who need transportation
26

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

to select locations in the Plan Area. Most households in
the NYRCR Plan Area, however, appear to have ample
access to personal automobile transportation.

Emergency Facilities
The NYRCR Plan Area has significant first responder
resources, including numerous fire houses with
generators for back-up power. This resource has a
secondary value during severe weather events; in
addition to the emergency services provided, fire
houses often serve as alternate shelter and social
services locations.

Projects with
Regional Significance
The Committee made a concerted effort to advance
projects in the Plan Area that complement those
identified in the Village of Washingtonville NYRCR Plan,
formulated in the first round of the NYRCR Program.
The Committee recognized that a regional approach
can positively affect change and create cost efficiencies
through collaboration. Infrastructure and stormwater

management initiatives affect the entire region, and
emergency and social services typically transcend
geopolitical boundaries.
Two projects identified in the Village of Washingtonville
NYRCR Plan impact the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan Area. These projects focus on the flooding issues
from the Moodna Creek Watershed that threaten the
viability of both the Town of Blooming Grove and Village
of Washingtonville.

Interagency Cooperation
As the Committee developed its list of projects,
members reached out to regional organizations to
discuss project ideas and opportunities for collaboration
and to identify any required regulatory approval
processes.
Assessing available resources was critical and included
datasets, feasibility assessments, and financial and
regulatory resources to support project implementation.
This collective process was supported and furthered
through coordination with the State Agency Review
Team (SART).

Section 2: Assessment of Risk and Needs
Description of Community
Assets and Assessment of Risk

A

primary goal of the Town of Blooming
Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction
(NYRCR) Plan is to ensure that both
reconstructed assets and proposed
post-storm construction projects are
more resilient during future storm events. The Town
of Blooming Grove NYRCR Planning Committee
(Committee) began to identify and analyze economic
drivers, health and social services, natural and cultural
resources, housing, and infrastructure assets in the
Town of Blooming Grove (Town) and the Town of
Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan Area (NYRCR Plan Area) to
better understand the community.
To meet this goal, the Committee developed a
comprehensive inventory of assets, both within and
beyond the NYRCR Plan Area. With the aid of this
inventory, the Committee compiled sufficient and
accurate information to assess the risk to assets under
current and future conditions in the planning horizon.
The Committee was actively engaged throughout the
inventory and risk assessment process, collaboratively
reviewing all aspects of the assessment, and collectively
approving the results.

Inventory Process
DATA COLLECTION
To kick off this process, the Committee prepared a
preliminary inventory of assets through stakeholder
outreach, Committee deliberations, and review of
datasets. The New York State Department of State
(NYS DOS) Risk Assessment Work Group facilitated
development of a comprehensive list of datasets
that were provided to the Committee. NYS DOS also
provided databases that included datasets from
numerous public and private sources. In addition to
SECTION 2: ASSESSMENT OF RISK AND NEEDS

these resources, the Committee compiled local-level
data from the Orange County Geographic Information
System (GIS) and the Town of Blooming Grove Hazard
Mitigation Plan (HMP) (2013). Data was also gathered
from Committee Members during scheduled Planning
Committee Meetings and via a community map portal,
as discussed in the Collaborative Approach to Public
Engagement strategy in the following section.

A COLLABORATIVE APPROACH
TO ENGAGING THE PUBLIC
GIS services often serve as a vital tool for communities
developing an inventory of structures, natural features,
or other assets associated with a physical location.
However, GIS services are frequently less available
to the suburban and rural areas of New York State,
compared to their urban counterparts. While it may
not be recorded in any discernible geographic format,
suburban and rural residents have a wealth of local
knowledge which could contribute to such an inventory.
The Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Community
(Community) and Committee sought to take advantage
of this local knowledge.

COMMUNITY MAP PORTAL
Planning Committee Meetings and widely-publicized
Public Engagement Events were held to capitalize
on local knowledge, create a collaborative dialogue
with Community members, and encourage increased
participation from local residents in the development
of the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan. The
Consultant Team, which assisted the Committee
throughout the project, incorporated all local knowledge
of critical community assets into an interactive GIS webmapping portal.

30

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

This unique online tool allowed the
Committee to identify, verify, and provide
details on Community assets and critical
facilities.
The community map portal also allowed Committee
Members to visualize, interact with, edit, and add assets
themselves, creating a greater sense of ownership in the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan’s development.
The portal allowed for the capture and use of local
knowledge to populate and refine the asset inventory
information as indicated in Figure 2.1.

Assets were additionally classified as either “critical”
or “non-critical” facilities. As defined by FEMA, critical
assets consist of features that create or extend the
useful life of structures, as well as facilities that provide
important community services. These assets include,
but are not limited to, healthcare facilities, emergency
operation centers and emergency response providers,
power generation facilities, and educational facilities.

ASSET CLASSIFICATION
Identified assets were reviewed and classified into six
categories, in accordance with the Federal Emergency
Management Agency (FEMA)’s National Disaster
Recovery Framework. These categories, along with
examples, are described in Table 2.1.

Description of Risk Areas
Once assets were identified and classified, it was
essential to understand which geographic areas have
been and will be affected by flooding to determine
which assets are at risk and to what degree. The three
risk areas for riverine communities are based on current
FEMA Flood Hazard Area (FHA) data, FEMA National
Flood Insurance Policy (NFIP) repetitive loss (RL) data,
and NFIP severe repetitive loss (SRL) data. These areas
reflect the frequency and likelihood of flood inundation
and are classified as “extreme,” “high,” or “moderate,”
in descending order of risk magnitude. Figure 2.2
provides a visual reference for the riverine risk areas in
the NYRCR Plan Area.
Because the NYRCR Plan Area includes a significant
amount of land outside of the riverine risk area where
past flood damage has caused repetitive damage to
residential properties, transportation infrastructure,
SECTION 2: ASSESSMENT OF RISK AND NEEDS

and emergency operations, the Committee decided to
include additional areas of “moderate” risk associated
with these historic damage areas.
In order to accurately classify assets in these areas, two
additional geographic risk areas were included in the
analysis. First, areas with a 1,000-foot radial buffer of
a past flood damage location were added. Second, a
new detailed layer of streams, creeks, and other minor
waterways in the NYRCR Plan Area was used to generate
additional risk areas for land within 1,000 feet of those
waterways. These additions to the overall risk area
helped the Committee paint a more accurate picture
of the frequency and likelihood for flood inundation
throughout the NYRCR Plan Area, rather than limiting
the analysis to only areas in the identified FEMA FHA.
The Committee highlighted several flood risk areas of
increased concern to the Community.

32

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Satterly Creek experiences two locations with significant
riverine flooding. These include the Barnes Road
subdivision, located upstream from the Horton Road
Bridge, and the Merriewold subdivision, particularly
along Merriewold Lake and upstream of the Lake.

Moodna Creek is also home to two
locations with problematic, repetitive
flooding experiences. Several houses
immediately downstream of Beaver Dam
Lake frequently flood where water from the
lake enters Moodna Creek, near Route 94.
The second area is along the western edge
of the Village of Washingtonville. While the
Village of Washingtonville experiences more
direct flooding in this area, the floodwaters
frequently affect transportation routes and
limit access to the Town of Blooming Grove
as well.

Lastly, overland flooding significantly
contributes to storm damage in the
Town’s communities.
Some communities on the slope of the Schunemunk
Mountain, such as the Mountain Lodge Park community,
were originally built as vacation cottage communities
with little infrastructure. Since being built, these
structures have been upgraded and are now inhabited
year-round, most typically by individuals with low
incomes. Despite the increase in full-time residency
levels, the stormwater culverts built into the dirt
roads and driveways in these communities have been
neglected. During storm events, water flows down
Schunemunk Mountain, into the homes, and prevents
emergency vehicle access to the area. Post-storm
damage also creates an economic burden for most
of these residents as well, due to the average lower
income of the area.

Many culverts in the NYRCR Plan Area are impeded by vegetation, such as the one pictured here. Image is courtesy of Eric Thayer.

Description of Community Assets
The following section describes the Town of Blooming
Grove’s identified assets by Recovery Support Function
(RSF) and provides additional information for each asset
group. The RSFs used here are adopted from FEMA’s
National Disaster Recovery Framework, where they
serve as the coordinating structure for key areas of
federal emergency assistance, as well as the structural
roadmap for this NYRCR Plan.
• Community Planning and Capacity Building
• Economic Development
• Health and Social Services
• Housing
• Infrastructure
• Natural and Cultural Resources
Figures 2.3 through 2.7 illustrate the location of assets
by RSFs in the NYRCR Plan Area and for the extent of the
defined risk area.

ECONOMIC ASSETS
The Town of Blooming Grove is located in the central
portion of Orange County, New York. The Town lies
in the Hudson Valley region of New York State, near
the foothills of the Shawangunk Mountains, a local
mountain range. Local industry expanded in the Town
during the 1700s, with mills using the Satterly Creek for
power. Many of the Town’s current residents commute
to the New York City metropolitan area, making the
Town a bedroom community. At present time, very
few agricultural jobs exist in Blooming Grove and only
within small, family-operated farms. Rather, the Town’s
economy is now focused on managerial, sales, and
service fields.
The Committee identified 53 facilities in this economic
category, including restaurants, gas stations, banks, and
grocery stores. A majority of these assets are actually
located along Routes 208 and 94, in and around the
Village of Washingtonville.
Although the Village of Washingtonville is not included
in the NYRCR Plan Area, the Committee still determined
these assets to be critical, because they support Town
residents in disaster situations by providing supplies,
food, and services.

Balancing open areas and farmland with developed land uses will be important to build economic resiliency, while protecting the natural
environment. Photo is courtesy of Eric Thayer.

HEALTH AND SOCIAL SERVICE ASSETS
This asset category includes services that provide a
variety of key public functions, from health treatment
facilities to general-purpose shelters in public schools,
and from post offices to town halls. During a flood
event, these facilities could serve as critical disaster
response and recovery centers, and identification of
these elements remains crucial to ensuring appropriate
future disaster management and preparedness. This
category also contains many critical assets, including fire
protection, police services, hospitals, and emergency
operations facilities.
During Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm Lee, storm
damage to parts of the Town of Blooming Grove
severely hindered residents’ ability to access health and
social services. In some cases, residents and business
owners were completely unable to access health and
social services, due to impassable and damaged roads
and bridges.
The Committee identified 33 facilities in this category,
including the Blooming Grove Ambulance Company; the
Washingtonville, Salisbury Mills, and South Blooming
Grove Fire Departments; and two hostels. Several of
these structures suffered flood damage from Hurricane
Irene and Tropical Storm Lee in 2011.

CRITICAL FACILITIES

Emergency medical personnel were not able to reach all residents
in need because of damaged roads. This type of situation can
be particularly perilous to vulnerable people. Photo is courtesy
of Tetra Tech, Inc.

Critical facilities are assets that the community relies on
for essential services. They include:
• Facilities that produce, use, or store highly volatile,
flammable, explosive, toxic, or water-reactive
materials;
• Hospitals, nursing homes, and housing for the
elderly. Occupants of these facilities may be unable
to evacuate during a flood or a severe storm event;
• Emergency operation centers or data storage
centers that contain records or services that may
be lost or inoperative during flood and storm
events; and
• Power-generating plants and other principal points
of utility lines.

INFRASTRUCTURE ASSETS
Infrastructure assets consist of resources such as
pedestrian, bicycle, and vehicular ways; transit; bridges;
airports; rail; gas stations; water supply structures;
stormwater, wastewater and solid waste management;
and recycling centers.

Roadways
Major roads in the Town include State Routes 17, 208,
and 94, along with County Routes 20 (Orrs Mill Road),
27 (Clove Road), 44 (Seven Springs Mountain Road/
Ridge Road), 51 (Hulsetown/Craigville Road), and 66
(Craigville Road). These corridors provide primary access
into and out of the Town. State Route 17 also provides
access to Interstates 84 and 87.

Transit
The Metropolitan Transit Authority (MTA) provides
commuter rail service linking Orange County to
New York City via the Port Jervis Line of the MetroNorth Commuter Railroad. While these regional
transportations are typically less vulnerable to impacts
from flooding, local residents depend on these modes
of transportation. Widespread flooding and debris
from Hurricane Irene rendered a Metro-North station
in Salisbury Mills, east of the Village of Washingtonville,

During major storm events, it is critically important to keep open
roads that provide major access into and out of Town. Photo is
courtesy of Eric Thayer.
39

completely useless for days after the storm. This storm
debris suspended operation of the entire Metro-North
line, significantly impacting commuters.

Public Utilities
Most residents of the Town rely on private wells for
their water. Additionally, although the rest rely on the
six municipal water supply districts, these districts also
use wells for their water supply. Therefore, all residents
of the Town rely on wells for their water supply.
Likewise, most residents use on-lot septic systems to
treat wastewater. Three wastewater treatment districts
(the Orange County Sewer District Number 1, the
Tappan Hill Sewer District, and the Glenwood Sewer
District) serve the remainder of the Town.
A total of 59 Town facilities were identified in this
category, including water plants, wells, communications
facilities, the Mayer Landfill, and numerous
transportation facilities.
The Town and Committee most frequently identified
transportation-related facilities as assets in this
category. Community and Committee feedback clearly
demonstrated the importance of safe access to and
from Town during storm events.

Protecting infrastructure assets, such as the Tappan Hill Wastewater
Treatment Plant at Barnes Road, is vital to maintaining sanitary
conditions during flooding. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

NATURAL AND CULTURAL RESOURCE ASSETS
Natural and cultural resources strategically enrich the
overall quality of life within the Town of Blooming
Grove. These resources provide scenic, recreational,
and environmental benefits, as well as protect other
community assets from potential flood impacts.
Identifying these assets is important to: (1) understand
ways to protect historic and cultural resources; and (2)
determine where natural resources, such as wetlands
or floodplains, could be enhanced to help protect the
Town’s infrastructure and other assets during storms.

Floodplains are lands that border rivers and
streams. Floodplains are normally dry, but
become covered with water during floods.
Buildings and structures in the floodplain
have a higher potential to be damaged by
floods. These structures can also change the
pattern of water flow and increase flooding
and damage on adjacent properties.

approximately 85 acres in the Town as Class 1 wetlands.
NYS DEC ranks wetlands according to their ability
to perform wetland functions and provide wetland
benefits, with Class 1 wetlands having the highest rank.
Another 671 acres of wetlands are spread throughout
the Town, mostly in the western half of the Town.
The Town is also home to other natural and manmade
cultural resources. Nine parks and recreational facilities,
along with several lakes and streams, provide a wide
range of recreational opportunities for the Town’s
residents. These facilities can accommodate an array of
activities, such as playground activities, picnics, cooking,
basketball, baseball, football, soccer, swimming, fishing,
waterskiing, bicycling, walking, jogging, and a place to
relax with friends and family.
The Committee and Community identified 77 facilities
as Natural and Cultural Resource Assets, including the
Town Hall, churches, community organization facilities,
parks, and numerous water bodies.

The Town of Blooming Grove consists of approximately
20,661 acres. Approximately 1,251 acres (5.9%) of
Town land resides in the 100-year FHA and about 350
acres (1.7%) of Town land in the 500-year FHA. These
floodplains are associated with the Moodna Creek
and its watershed, which includes the Cromline Creek,
Satterly Creek, and Perry Creek. Numerous buildings
and structures are located in both the 100-year and
500-year floodplains.
Wetlands function as natural sponges that trap
and slowly release surface water, rain, snowmelt,
groundwater, and floodwaters. Trees, root mats,
and other wetland vegetation slow the speed of
floodwaters, distributing them slowly over the
floodplain. A one-acre wetland can typically store
about one million gallons of water.
Overall, wetlands protect adjacent and downstream
properties from flood damage. By preserving and
protecting these natural features, they help reduce
storm damage from weather events. The New York
State Department of Environmental Conservation
(NYS DEC) has designated 23 wetland areas in the
Town of Blooming Grove. The NYS DEC has classified
