HOUSING
The Town has several challenges affecting the
provision of safe and adequate housing for current and
future residents. These challenges were significantly
exacerbated during and after the floods from Hurricane
Irene and Tropical Storm Lee.
A total of 12 facilities were identified in this category.
This asset category includes, but is not limited to, multifamily dwellings, supportive housing/group homes,
and senior housing. Eleven of the 12 facilities are multifamily residences, e.g., apartments, many of which
serve vulnerable populations. Several of the multi-family
residences are clustered in the north-central part of
Town, as indicated in Figure 2.7.

In addition to the areas shown on the risk map in Figure
2.7, several older residential areas experience significant
overland flooding issues. These include the Mountain
Lodge Park community and others communities on the
slopes of Schunemunk Mountain. These residential
areas were developed in the 1960s and 1970s as
vacation cottages and contained little infrastructure.
The roads and driveways are not paved, and years of
stormwater flowing down the mountain have damaged
or destroyed the stormwater culverts. During storm
events, overland floodwaters flow into homes, and
emergency vehicles are cut off from these areas.

NYRCR: Town of Blooming Grove, Orange County
FIGURE 2.7 – HOUSING ASSETS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Assessment of Risk to
Assets and Systems
The Town of Blooming Grove has suffered recent
damage by the repetitive flooding of the Moodna
Creek. The non-flood stage water velocities along
Moodna Creek move fairly slowly, increasing sediment
accumulation behind bridges and spillways, which then
leads to an increased chance of flooding. As Moodna
Creek’s main channel is rather shallow for most of its
course throughout the Town, flood conditions develop
rapidly during periods of heavy rain. Confluences of
tributaries and culverts may also impact the flood
conditions of Moodna Creek.

The proximity of the Town to Moodna Creek
and its ongoing streambank erosion lead to
the primary causes of the Town’s substantial
flood risk.
According to the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers (USACE),
the primary causes of flooding include:
• Reduced Stream Capacity – Continuous bank
erosion has led to the accumulation of sediment in
Moodna Creek. Bank erosion and sediment buildup contribute to changing stream conditions, the
loss of vegetation, and a reduced channel capacity,
leading to flooding and community damage.
• Debris and Other Obstructions – Obstructions
in the stream impede the flow of water and
contribute to increased flood risk for the Town.

Flooding Overview –
HEC-RAS Modeling
As discussed in Section 1, the Town of Blooming Grove
is located in a mountainous territory characterized
by the presence of several streams and lakes. The
main watercourses flowing through the community
are Cromline Creek, Perry Creek, and Satterly Creek,
tributaries of Moodna Creek that enter in the Village
of Washingtonville. Detailed Hydrologic Engineering
Centers–River Analysis System (HEC-RAS) models are
47

available for Perry Creek and Satterly Creek. A separate
hydraulic analysis of Moodna Creek had been previously
conducted for the assessment of flood mitigation
projects in Washingtonville (for details, please refer to
the Village of Washingtonville NYRCR Plan).
Flooding within the Town of Blooming Grove is primarily
caused by overflow of Moodna Creek’s and Satterly
Creek’s banks, given the shallowness of their main
channel. The water velocities along Moodna Creek and
Satterly Creek are fairly slow, increasing the potential
of sediment accumulation behind bridges, culverts,
and spillways, and therefore increasing the chance for
flooding. Similarly, flooding issues along Perry Creek
are possibly related to backwater effects in proximity of
inline weirs. Confluences of tributaries may also have
impacts on flood condition of the creeks.
In addition, localized flooding due to stormwater
runoff has affected some areas throughout the
community, particularly in steeper areas like those
found in Mountain Lodge Park. Flooding risk in all
areas is exacerbated by the minimal amount of natural
flood storage in the watershed due to the geology and
topography of the area.
Based on the extension of the FEMA 100- and 500-year
floodplain and the hydraulic analysis conducted through
the HEC-RAS models, the main areas of the Blooming
Grove community that are most prone to flooding are:
the Satterly Creek section between Round Hill Road and
Horton Road; the area surrounding the Hudson Road
bridge on Satterly Creek; the area surrounding the Perry
Creek inline weir at Felter Hill Road; and the Perry Creek
section between Mountain Lodge Road and Woodcock
Mountain Road. The community indicated additional
areas that flood during major storm events, but for
which no HEC-RAS models are currently available for
the analysis, including: Mountain Lodge Park, which is
prone to stormwater runoff flooding from Schunemunk
Mountain; the Satterly Creek culvert at Prospect Road;
Turtle Knoll Road, downstream of Lake Hildegarde;
and the area surrounding Merriewold Lake in South
Blooming Grove.

SECTION 2: ASSESSMENT OF RISK AND NEEDS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Due to a lack of existing HEC-RAS models for these
locations, the Committee recommends further analysis
of these sites to be conducted, using alternative
methods, such as: estimating the flood storage capacity
of potential retention areas; and assessing the capacity
of existing culverts or stormwater drainage systems.
Satterly Creek and Perry Creek consist of multiple
arms and several minor tributaries; although their
flow contribution is included in the available HEC-RAS
models, physical geometry is restricted to only the main
channels. Bank erosion along Satterly Creek and Perry
Creek, as well as these minor tributaries might have
increased the amount of sediment deposited upstream
of bridges, culverts, and spillways, leading to additional
local flood risks.
Critical buildings and facilities were identified within
the Satterly Creek and Perry Creek floodplains. Most of
these structures include residential buildings, as well
as the Tappan Wastewater Treatment Plant, which is
located in the Barnes Road neighborhood. These assets
may require flood protection measures or relocation to
remove them from the existing floodplain. The impact
of flooding and the amount of protection required is
assessed on a case-by-case basis, using the hydraulic
modeling analysis.
The Town hydraulic analysis focused on Satterly Creek
and Perry Creek, and did not include study of past
conditions to determine whether changes in the
watershed, such as increased impervious surfaces,
would increase flooding problems. A desktop and field
reconnaissance of Blooming Grove did not identify any
large new development areas that might affect the
nature of the local hydrology, but climate change effects
could result in larger design storms, indicating the
existing stormwater system as undersized.
Many of the potential flood mitigation projects and
recommendations for funding through the NYRCR
Program assessed by the Committee are informed
by the results of the HEC-RAS modeling results. For
instance, stormwater drainage improvements are being
considered for Mountain Lodge Park. The installation of
an earthen levee along the Satterly Creek’s right bank is
being evaluated in terms of floodplain extent reduction.
SECTION 2: ASSESSMENT OF RISK AND NEEDS

Furthermore, based on existing model availability,
additional hydrologic and hydraulic analysis is
recommended to evaluate the potential for modification
of the existing lakes in the watershed, i.e., OrangeRockland, Merriewold, Hildegarde, and Tomahawk, or
the identification of new retention areas to provide
additional flood storage and therefore potentially
reduce impacts downstream along Satterly Creek and
Moodna Creek.

Description of Methodology
The NYS DOS provided the Town with a Risk Assessment
Tool that determined risk levels for the Town’s assets,
based on Community and Committee feedback and
in conjunction with information captured by the asset
inventory. The Risk Assessment Tool is designed to
assess and quantify the risk to individual community
assets through built-in formulas that calculate an
overall risk score category based on three factors:
hazard, exposure, and vulnerability. The tool calculates
a score for each of these factors and combines the
scores to represent the relative risk of each asset in the
community to one another.
The Risk Assessment Tool calculation combines scores
for the three factors shown in the formula below. Each
factor in this equation is calculated automatically, based
on appropriate inputs and are assigned as follows:
HAZARD
x EXPOSURE
x VULNERABILITY
= RISK
• HAZARD SCORE: Assigned for each asset based on
a 100-year storm event occurring within the next
100 years.
• EXPOSURE SCORE: Determined by the risk area
where the asset is located and by local landscape
attributes that influence the potential for storm
impacts. This score reflects how landscape features
can moderate damage to individual assets.

48

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

• VULNERABILITY SCORE: Reflects the level
of impairment or consequences that assets may
experience from a hazard event, and reflects
the ability of the asset to resist damage from
the hazard.

INTERPRETATION OF RISK
Risk scores help to identify assets with an elevated
potential for storm damage. Some factors that could
be considered for each asset when developing a
community risk management strategy include:
• Contribution to life safety;
• Whether the asset is a critical facility;
• Value of asset to the community;
• Environmental services provided;

priority based on exposure or because vulnerability
remains relatively low. It is recommended that a
combination of measures could be considered to
reduce exposure or vulnerability.

Residual Category

Risk scores in the residual category occur when both
exposure and vulnerability remain relatively low.
This situation suggests floods would pose minor or
infrequent consequences. Note that risk is never
completely eliminated. Some residual risk still
remains, even after management measures have
been implemented. It is recommended to monitor
conditions and adapt, as necessary.

For further information regarding risk scores, refer to
Section 5: Additional Materials.

• Economic contribution of the asset;
• Whether alternatives are available; and
• Capacity of the asset to adapt.
The Consultant Team evaluated risk for a 100-year event
(1% annual chance) and a 500-year event (0.2% annual
chance), both of which represent higher-intensity storm
events. Risk was calculated for each asset and each
asset was placed in one of the following categories.

Severe Category

If possible, both exposure and vulnerability could
be reduced for assets in this category. Relocation of
these assets could be considered as a priority option.

FLOODPLAIN VS. FLOODWAY
A floodplain is defined as the low lands adjoining
the channel of a river, stream, or watercourse, or
adjoining the shore of an ocean, lake, or other body
of standing water, which have been inundated by
flood water. Floodplains are further categorized by
the frequency of flooding. Most often, floodplains
are referred to as 100-year floodplains.
A regulatory floodway refers to the channel of a
river or other watercourse and the adjacent land
areas that must be reserved to discharge the base
flood without cumulatively increasing the water
surface elevation more than a designated height.
Communities must regulate development in these
floodways to ensure there are no increases in
upstream flood elevations.

High Category

Risk scores in the high category indicate conditions
that could lead to significant negative outcomes from
a storm event.
Actions could be taken to reduce vulnerability, such
as elevating or flood-proofing the asset to help avoid
a long-term loss of function.

Moderate Category

Risk scores in this category pose moderate-to-serious
consequences, but adaptation may be a lower

49

Assessment Results
Many assets identified in the Town are along the
Moodna, Cromline, and Satterly Creeks. Flooding of
Moodna Creek poses the greatest risk to the Town and
many of its key assets. Stronger and more frequent large
storms have rendered the Town of Blooming Grove
more susceptible to the devastating effects of flooding.
Clearly, many assets in the Town are exposed and

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

extremely vulnerable to storm events, and the risk score
results below confirm this vulnerability.

Not all assets vulnerable to severe and
repetitive flooding are located in the
regulatory 100-year floodplain or identified
“Risk Area.” Many assets outside these
areas experienced significant damages
during Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm
Lee, storms that caused flooding exceeding
most projected expectations.

risk scores were generated for assets outside of an

susceptible and at-risk to flooding events.

To reduce future flood damages and losses to these

Infrastructure Assets
Based on information provided by the public and
Committee, one infrastructure asset, i.e., the South
Blooming Grove Wastewater facility, was determined
to have severe risk from flooding, 22 assets were
identified at high risk from flooding, and six identified
at moderate risk from flooding during a 100-year event.
The remaining infrastructure assets were identified to
have residual risk.
Seventeen of the high risk assets are roadway bridges
over waterways. Floodwaters may rise above the
bridges, cutting off roadway access. Floodwaters can
also cause damage to the bridges. The other identified
high risk assets consist of water supply or wastewater
treatment facilities. Damage to these facilities may
impact public health. Potable water supplies may
be cut off from residents who are dependent on
municipal water services. Wastewater may also be
carried from the treatment plants by floodwaters and
spread throughout the flooded area, contaminating
it with harmful sewage. Many of these infrastructure
assets are considered critical facilities, have high value
to the Community, and/or serve socially vulnerable
populations.
Even greater numbers of infrastructure assets have
been routinely impacted by flood events, especially
the flood events associated with Hurricane Irene and
Tropical Storm Lee. Table 2.4 lists the risk scores for
infrastructure assets in the Town of Blooming Grove.

Housing Assets

Most housing assets in the Town are outside the areas
at risk from riverine flooding; however, they may be
at risk of overland flooding as a result of inadequate
stormwater management structures and practices.
This risk is not reflected in the risk analysis results
presented in this section. The facilities listed in
Table 2.6 could be prioritized for flood-proofing or
other mitigation measures to reduce both vulnerability
and exposure.

One housing asset, i.e., the Rolling Hills Condo complex,
was identified as having moderate risk from flooding
during a 100-year or 500-year event. This ranking is
consistent with information provided by the Community
and Committee. The Rolling Hills Condos are located
in the 0.2% annual chance floodplain and are situated
approximately 300 feet west of Satterly Creek. Table 2.6
lists the risk scores for housing assets in the Town of
Blooming Grove.

SECTION 2: ASSESSMENT OF RISK AND NEEDS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Assessment of Needs
and Opportunities
The Committee identified preliminary needs and
opportunities for the Town of Blooming Grove,
categorized by each of the six RSFs that serve as the
structural roadmap for this NYRCR Plan.

Community Planning and
Capacity Building
The Community Planning and Capacity Building RSF
addresses the community’s ability to implement storm
recovery activities and to mitigate the effects of future
storms. The Town’s experience with recent major
storms demonstrated that the existing stormwater
management system is ineffective and that compliance
with community regulations (such as clearing streams
and drainage ways on private property) needs to be
increased.
The Town of Blooming Grove updated its Hazard
Mitigation Plan in 2013. This document assisted the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR planning process, as
it described the impacts of the storms and identified
actions that could be taken to increase the Town’s
resiliency from future storms and flood events.

COMMUNITY PLANNING AND
CAPACITY BUILDING
NEEDS AND OPPORTUNITIES
Need: Develop and enforce local zoning and codes

that help secure public safety, preserve property, and limit
disaster impacts.

Opportunity: Enforce local codes to ensure water
quality and prevent unregulated effluent from infiltrating the
water courses.
Opportunity: Adopt impervious coverage requirements in
all zoning districts but especially in the Rural Crossroads (RC)-I
and RC-II Districts (a 50% impervious coverage maximum is in
place in the Office Research/Light Industrial [ORI] District).
Opportunity: Modify site plan and zoning standards to
encourage and incentivize the use of low-impact development
and sustainable design techniques, including porous pavement
systems, in all zoning districts (a modest density bonus is in place
for major subdivisions in the Rural Residential [RR] District).
Need: Improve local capabilities to conduct floodplain

management, reduce flood vulnerability, and reduce the burden
of flood insurance on property owners.

Opportunity: Support participation in the NFIP Community
Rating System (CRS).
Opportunity: Develop a town-wide strategy to issue current
elevation certificates.
Need: Limit building in the floodplain.
Need: Increase public education and outreach for residents,
especially those most vulnerable to natural disasters.

Opportunity: Provide counseling services to

residents regarding FEMA Letters of Map Amendment (LOMA),
and other outreach and education about FEMA NFIP compliance.

Opportunity: Create emergency action/response plans

and associated guidelines for residents and emergency service
agencies to follow after a flood event.

SECTION 2: ASSESSMENT OF RISK AND NEEDS

62

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Economic Development
The primary economic concern after a disaster is
returning economic and business activities to a state of
health. Recovery periods present unique opportunities
for developing new economic strengths that result
in a more sustainable and fiscally robust community.
Communities that deliberately design an economic
development strategy, and support these elements in
their planning process, are more likely to capitalize on
opportunities for economic improvement, such as
those presented through recovery programs like the
NYRCR Program.
With this in mind, the Committee enumerated the
following needs and recommendations that focus on
protecting existing homes and businesses to withstand
flood impacts.

ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENT
NEEDS AND OPPORTUNITIES

that of care outside of flood events.
During storm events, communications systems
frequently become compromised, so messages to
residents regarding recommended actions may not be
received. The Committee noted that most residents are
not well-informed about what to do in preparation for
an impending storm or what to do during a flood.

Opportunity: Flood proof or relocate critical facilities
and assets.

Opportunity: Identify businesses suitable for flood

interruptions to critical public health, safety, and
communication services underlies a number of
the needs and opportunities the Committee
identified below.

HEALTH AND SOCIAL SERVICES
NEEDS AND OPPORTUNITIES

Health and Social Services
After a disaster, one of the more immediate
considerations is whether public health, healthcare
facilities, and essential social service needs have
been restored. To develop appropriate strategies and
management measures, the Committee reviewed
the existing Town assets that support vulnerable
populations and identified key needs critical to
protecting the health and well-being of all residents in a
post-disaster environment. Transportation infrastructure
failures and access interruption consist of the main
63

Need: Maintain access to healthcare services during and

after disasters, including uninterrupted emergency and medical
services during flood events.

Need: Ensure that adequate infrastructure and connectivity
exists within the community to permit safe and efficient
provision of services.

Opportunity: Conduct a disaster and flood preparedness
outreach and education campaign to residents, especially to
functional needs residents and other vulnerable populations.

Need: Create new shelter facilities in the Town.
Opportunity: Ensure continuous operation of critical

emergency response personnel and facilities during future
flood events.

Housing

Infrastructure

Local housing goals emphasize protecting housing
assets from flood damages and providing resources
to residents to better prepare for the effects of future
storms. Resiliency alternatives are directed toward:

As indicated in Figure 1.4 in Section 1, flooding from
Tropical Storm Lee and Hurricane Irene caused the
closure of substantial sections of the Town’s major
roadways, including U.S. Route 6/State Route 17, State
Route 94, State Route 208, Clove Road, Farmingdale
Road, Woodcock Mountain Drive, Station Road, and
Prospect Road.

(1) the types and locations of housing assets with the
greatest needs; (2) the provision of sufficient housing
alternatives for owners and renters; and (3) the
implementation of incentive programs for
homeowners to undertake home elevations or other
mitigation retrofits.
As people move from the urbanized areas around New
York City to the Town of Blooming Grove, they may not
be aware of the Town’s susceptibility to flooding both
around the waterways and on hillside slopes or what
that susceptibility means for them. Opportunities exist
to educate residents about flood risks to their homes,
the NFIP, and insurance rates.
Working from an intimate knowledge of these factors,
the Committee identified housing-related needs and
opportunities necessary for further evaluation in the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR planning process.

HOUSING
NEEDS AND OPPORTUNITIES
Need: Address cost-burdened residents.
Need: Protect existing housing stock from further loss.
Opportunity: Identify homes suitable for flood mitigation

improvements, including elevation, flood-proofing, and potential
acquisition.

Opportunity: Create a program to offer or assist with
voluntary home and property buy-outs.

Need: Create a strategy that protects existing housing stock
and reduces flood insurance rates.

Opportunity: Offer education and consultation to area
residents on FEMA flood insurance requirements.

Many undersized and blocked culverts resulted in
roadway flooding, cutting off access for emergency
vehicles and stranding many people in their homes with
no way to get necessary supplies and assistance. Dirt
roads in the Mountain Lodge Park and other hillside
communities had washed out, cutting off access to
many residents.
During Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm Lee, the
Tappan Water and Wastewater Plants were damaged
and made inaccessible by floodwaters. Heavy rains and
strong winds from the storms knocked down trees and
power lines across a wide area.
The Blooming Grove Committee’s infrastructure needs
and opportunities focus on improving stormwater
management infrastructure and protecting the
transportation network. HEC-RAS modeling may
identify elements (such as specific culverts, bridges,
or roadways) that could be addressed to protect the
Town’s infrastructure.

INFRASTRUCTURE
NEEDS AND OPPORTUNITIES
Need: Improve infrastructure capacity and
stormwater management.

Opportunity: Strengthen floodplain management

throughout the Town by improving infrastructure capacity and
stormwater management. For instance, add/improve drainage
capacity along major roadways in Town.

Opportunity: Create retention ponds and/or
flood storage.

Need: Repair, replace, and enhance culverts, dams, and
spillways to address repetitive flooding.

Natural and Cultural Resources
Naturally occurring infrastructure has been
increasingly recognized and promoted among hazard
and climate planners and local government as a lowimpact and sustainable means to mitigate losses from
natural hazards. The substantial flood risk in the Town
of Blooming Grove is primarily caused by the proximity
of development to Moodna Creek and other creeks
in the Town. The Committee identified a number of
needs and opportunities capitalizing on these assets to
integrate conservation and property protection. These
needs and opportunities follow.

repetitive flood areas.

Section 3

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Section 3:
Reconstruction and
Resiliency Strategies

T

hrough a combination of additional analysis,
ongoing discussions at Town of Blooming
Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction
(NYRCR) Committee (Committee) Meetings,
and public feedback, the Committee
identified a series of preliminary strategies to address
the most critical needs related to community health,
safety, resiliency, and quality of life.

The strategies reflect community values,
issues, needs, and opportunities, and they
serve as the foundation for identifying and
prioritizing projects and implementation
actions in Section 4 of this NYRCR Plan.
The following discussion includes many of the needs
identified by the Committee and presented in Section
2: Assessment of Risk and Needs to help draw the
connection between those needs and the related
strategies. Some of those needs and opportunities
appear explicitly in one or more strategies, either by
being referenced in a strategy title or by being called

out in the strategy description. Others are presented
as actions in the associated tables after each
strategy description.
The Committee developed these strategies with input
from the public. Section 4: Proposed and Featured
Project Profiles contains descriptions and cost-benefit
analyses of the proposed and featured projects. A full
list of Proposed, Featured, and Additional Resiliency
Recommendations is in Section 5: Additional Materials.

Strategy 1
Reduce the impact of riverine flooding on the built
environment, including critical facilities, infrastructure,
businesses, and housing.
The heavy rains and flooding from Hurricane Irene
and Tropical Storm Lee severely impacted many local
structures. Because of the extent of damage to the built
environment, the Committee first focused on reducing
these impacts in future storms. This strategy addresses
several needs identified in Section 2 of this NYRCR Plan.
The Committee recognized that one way to reduce
the impact of flooding on the built environment is
to develop and enforce strong controls, such as local
zoning and building codes, to secure public safety
and property.
The Community considers it a priority to explore
activities to minimize losses to existing houses in
flood hazard areas (FHA), especially those that have
experience repetitive loss (RL) or severe repetitive
loss (SRL). Some ways to achieve this goal include the
diversion of floodwaters to retention ponds, flood
storage areas, or other structural elements designed
to protect many homes at once or the reduction of
vulnerability by acquiring, elevating, or flood-proofing
individual homes. The Community can implement
similar actions to protect commercial structures.

This is an example of housing stock on Barnes Road. Photo is courtesy
of Tetra Tech, Inc.
SECTION 3: RECONSTRUCTION AND RESILIENCY STRATEGIES

68

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Such protection increases the likelihood that
businesses located inside these buildings
will remain in the Town of Blooming Grove,
rather than relocate to less flood-prone
areas outside of the Town.
The Committee has assessed and refined potential
flood mitigation projects and recommendations for
funding through the NYRCR Program to determine
which projects will best meet the Town of Blooming
Grove NYRCR Plan Area’s (NYRCR Plan Area) needs. This
strategy also addresses how to minimize the impacts of
future storms on critical facilities.
Repairing, replacing, or enhancing existing culverts,
dams, and spillways can address repetitive flooding
concerns. The Committee also identified the
coordination of the volume of water behind dams as
a potentially useful tool for managing flooding. These
benefits, however, must first be evaluated through a
complex hydraulic model. The results of such a study
could help the Town manage flooding, by directing
investment towards the most beneficial actions.
Recommendations from the Town of Blooming Grove
NYRCR Plan may also include restoring local lakes to

their original design depth and capacity or creating new
locations for flood storage along riverine waterways
at specific locations in Town. This NYRCR Plan will also
determine the value of using a coordinated release of
stormwater from behind local dams as a way to lessen
the impact of flooding downstream.
Protective measures and emergency rescues cannot be
carried out, unless homes and critical facilities remain
accessible during and after a flood. The installation of
an earthen levee along the Satterly Creek’s right bank
would protect residential buildings and critical assets
along the Barnes Road neighborhood; this option is
being evaluated through the Hydrologic Engineering
Centers–River Analysis System (HEC-RAS) modeling.
Initial results show positive impacts in terms of
floodplain extent reduction without increasing the
water surface elevation (WSE) significantly. It would also
reduce flood water inundation at the nearby Tappan
Waste Water Treatment Plant.
In light of these needs, this strategy to reduce the
impacts from flooding calls for investment in a range
of resiliency projects. Table 3.1 itemizes the projects
highlighted by the Committee to accomplish Strategy 1.

Past floods caused costly damages to local roadways and other infrastructure, and often led to route closures that lasted months or years after the
storm passes. Photo is courtesy of Committee Member Rich Bachman.

Barnes Road
Levee

This project proposes to create a berm/levee behind the residences
on Barnes Road, from 144 Barnes Road to the Satterly Creek side of
Tappan Sewer Plant, to deflect and keep the floodwaters away from
the homes. Instead, the structural project would direct floodwaters to
the non-residential side of Satterly Creek.

N

Community
Rating
System (CRS)
Participation

This project would fund professional services to develop an
application for the Town of Blooming Grove, potentially in partnership
with the Village of South Blooming Grove, to join the NFIP CRS to
reduce flood insurance rates in the Town. The project would aim to
enter participating municipalities into the program at a CRS Rating
of 8, which would immediately translate into a 10% discount in flood
insurance policy premiums for policy holders in good standing. In
addition to gaining membership to the CRS program, this contract
would educate the present Town Supervisor, Board Members, and the
public about the CRS program and its associated benefits; and assist
residents with resiliency-focused residential property improvements
and with access and awareness to needed funding resources.

SECTION 3: RECONSTRUCTION AND RESILIENCY STRATEGIES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Strategy 2
Improve stormwater management and drainage
systems throughout the town to decrease risk for
homes, businesses, and residents.
In addition to the damages caused by riverine flooding,
which are addressed in Strategy 1, overland sheet flow
and man-made interruptions to the natural drainage
process contributed to the damage of many structures.
The existing stormwater management systems in many
residential neighborhoods throughout the Town were
incapable of handling the volume of rain from the
storms. To address this, the Committee recognized the
need to improve the Town’s stormwater management
system and the capacity of its stormwater infrastructure.
The Committee evaluated stormwater drainage
improvements for Mountain Lodge Park, based on best
management practices outlined in New York State’s
technical guidelines and recommendations. It ultimately
recommended additional hydrologic and hydraulic
analysis to evaluate the potential for modification of the
existing lakes in the watershed (e.g., Orange-Rockland,
Merriewold, Hildegarde, and Tomahawk).
Alternatively, the analysis could identify new retention
areas to provide additional flood storage and potentially
reduce impacts downstream along Satterly and Moodna
Creeks. The Committee has also considered adding or
improving drainage along densely populated residential
roadways in the Town, where multiple residents would
benefit from the improvements.
Recognizing the Town of Blooming Grove’s limited
funding and operational capacity as a small, rural,
municipal government, the Committee recommended
the creation of a Detailed Inspection Program and
Investment Prioritization Program. This Program would
evaluate return on investment and improve economic
efficiency, resource allotment, and budgeting decisions

SECTION 3: RECONSTRUCTION AND RESILIENCY STRATEGIES

Strategy 3
Improve upon existing capabilities for
emergency preparedness, response, sheltering,
and communication.
This strategy focuses on improving the government’s
and residents’ capability to respond to future storms,
so that those impacts do not carry the same level
of damage as those of Superstorm Sandy, Hurricane
Irene, and Tropical Storm Lee. This strategy stresses
the needs and opportunities related to the Health and
Social Services Recovery Support Function (RSF) and
the Community Planning and Capacity Building RSF
identified by the Committee and described in Section 2.
Specifically, the Committee identified three needs
related to this strategy:
• Maintain access to health services during and after
disasters, including uninterrupted emergency and
medical services;
• Ensure adequate infrastructure and connectivity
exist in the Community to permit safe and efficient
provision of services; and
• Create new shelter facilities in the Town.

Together, these three items demonstrate
that the Committee is committed to
ensuring the Town of Blooming Grove and
the Village of South Blooming Grove, along
with their residents, receive all necessary
emergency assistance during a flood event
by improving existing emergency services.
The Committee recognized the opportunity to create
emergency action/response plans and associated
guidelines that residents and emergency service
agencies can follow after a flood or other hazard
event. These plans, and the planning process used to
develop them, will result in more effective, coordinated
responses by emergency responders.

SECTION 3: RECONSTRUCTION AND RESILIENCY STRATEGIES

A key part of response is the ability for emergency
responders and critical facilities to be fully operational
during a flood event. In the past, floodwaters damaged
equipment in firehouses, and town-wide power outages
hindered operations for numerous critical operations
centers lacking back-up power.
The Committee identified a number of facilities that
need enhancements to minimize their vulnerability to
future flood and power outages. These facilities include
the South Blooming Grove Village Hall, numerous fire
stations, the Town of Blooming Grove police station, and
designated local shelters.

Installing back-up power generation at all
identified critical facilities will help to ensure
that emergency response operations can
continue during flood events.
In addition to meeting the needs of emergency services
operations, the Committee recognized the importance
of an educated and prepared public for successfully
weathering future disaster events. Residents need to
know the best course of action to take when preparing
for a hazard event, how to find real-time flood and
disaster information, and where to go when evacuation
is necessary. Therefore, the Committee recommended
the development of guidelines for residents in
coordination with an extensive public disaster and flood
preparedness outreach and education campaign. These
guidelines and the outreach campaign will include
and address persons with access and functional needs
(PAFN) along with other vulnerable populations.

Committee to accomplish Strategy 3.

Strategy 4
Preserve, protect, and enhance the town’s natural,
recreational, and cultural resources, and restore
natural floodplain capabilities.
This strategy addresses the Natural and Cultural
Resources, Economic Development, and Community
Planning and Capacity Building RSFs. The Committee
recognizes that flooding is a natural process and that the
Town should leverage its natural resources to minimize
damage from future flood events. The Committee
also recognizes the need to integrate environmental
conservation with the protection of community homes,
assets, and infrastructure while addressing stream
conditions that cause or contribute to repeat floods.
The projects necessary to fulfill Strategy 4, detailed in
Table 3.4, leverage previously identified opportunities
to meet existing needs. The Town may create a
Master Plan that addresses problematic waterways
SECTION 3: RECONSTRUCTION AND RESILIENCY STRATEGIES

and sets forth a strategy to restore flood capacity
throughout the area.
Flood capacity restoration activities will address stream
conditions and may seek to alleviate silt deposits,
streambank erosion, and the build-up of both natural
and man-made debris. All of these conditions can
decrease a waterway’s capacity to access its natural
floodplain, strengthening the probability of flooding
in developed areas and thereby strengthening the
probability of costly structural damage.

This strategy also seeks to combine
improvements in emergency response and
preparedness with the creation of new
cultural and recreational amenities.
A multi-modal or pedestrian evacuation plan, for
instance, may be coupled with the creation of a local
recreational trail map. Acquiring and restoring a
stretch of eroding abandoned railroad bed that may be
76

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

contributing large amounts of silt to local waterways
could lead to a new multi-purpose recreational trail.
In addition to reducing flood damages to the built
environment and providing new recreational and
cultural assets, the Committee identified the protection
of water quality as a priority. The Town of Blooming
Grove maintains a municipal separate storm sewer
system (MS4). The Town oversees the health, safety, and
general welfare of its residents through the regulation
of discharges to this system.
Local codes and permitting requirements exist to
protect water quality by controlling the introduction
of pollutants into the MS4 system and to prevent
unregulated effluent from infiltrating the water courses.
The Committee recognized the NYRCR Plan as an
opportunity to refocus the Community on enforcing
MS4 requirements. The Committee also hopes to

promote public awareness of the potential hazards and
water quality impacts from improper discharge of trash,
yard waste, wastewater, sediment, and other pollutants.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Strategy 5
Assist Town Residents, Officials, and Business Owners
in Becoming Better Protected from Future Flood
Damages Through Education, Programming, Outreach,
and Policy Initiatives.
This strategy focuses on the Town’s regulatory
and technical capabilities to conduct floodplain
management. It focuses on addressing two needs in
particular. These needs include: (1) improving local
capabilities to conduct floodplain management, reduce
flood vulnerability, and reduce the burden of flood
insurance on property owners; and (2) reducing the cost
of doing business (e.g., flood insurance).
Participation by the Town of Blooming Grove in the
CRS, a voluntary program administered by the FEMA
NFIP, provides one approach to overcome Strategy
5 needs. The CRS recognizes and rewards those
community floodplain management activities exceeding
minimum NFIP standards by discounting flood insurance
premium rates.

Under the CRS program, the Community
would be rewarded for its interest in
developing a comprehensive approach
to floodplain management.
The issuance and maintenance of elevation certificates
comprise one component of earning CRS points.
This aligns with the identified opportunity to develop
a town-wide strategy to issue current elevation
certificates. Another element of the CRS refers to
community education and outreach. The Committee
has already identified the opportunity to educate area
residents on FEMA flood insurance requirements.
A third, related opportunity concerns the provision of
counseling services to residents regarding FEMA Letters
of Map Amendment (LOMA) and other outreach and
education about FEMA NFIP compliance.

Committee to complete Strategy 5.

PROPOSED PROJECTS

Section 4: Proposed and
Featured Project Profiles
Additional Hydrologic (Flood
Routing), Hydraulic, and
Stormwater Analysis
Project Background
Although the current level of analysis has been effective
in painting a broad picture of the needs, vulnerabilities,
and opportunities for improvement in the Town of
Blooming Grove, the Town of Blooming Grove NY
Rising Community (NYRCR) Committee (Committee)
recommends a comprehensive hydrologic and hydraulic
analysis of the Satterly, Perry, Cromline, and Moodna
Creeks. This analysis should extend to all relevant
tributaries and bodies of water outside of the Town
boundaries, where necessary, in addition to an analysis
of select stormwater flood damage areas.

More detailed local modeling would provide
further assessment of the known flooding
areas currently outside of existing hydraulic
models. Additionally, a regional, dynamic
hydrologic and hydraulic analysis would
identify additional potential flood storage
areas, and establish a lake management
plan to mitigate downstream flooding.
The analysis should include a comprehensive study
of all local waterways, lakes, and dams affecting flood
conditions within the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan Area (NYRCR Plan Area).

Orange and Rockland Lake Spillway. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

Other components of the study would include:
• Inspection of the numerous bridges and
structures that constrict flow;
• Identification and analysis of appropriate locations
throughout the Moodna Creek watershed to
provide additional flood storage. These locations
are in addition to those already mentioned in
other projects;
• Dynamic flood routing investigation to develop a
lake management plan to reduce water surface
elevations (WSE) in flood retention areas in
advance of large storm events;
• Analysis of the critical stormwater issues
affecting residents of the Schunemunk Mountain
neighborhoods;
• Identification and analysis of appropriate stream
management improvement measures, in addition
to those already mentioned in other projects; and
• Coordination with the Village of Washingtonville
NYRCR Plan implementation of a similar riverine
analysis project.
This comprehensive riverine and overland (stormwater)
hydraulic analyses will supplement currently insufficient
information and will provide recommendations to be

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

82

PROPOSED PROJECTS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

used in the planning and development of further actions
to reduce the extent and severity of flooding throughout
Town. This analysis would provide a platform for the
Town to implement final recommendations from
the study, as funds would be set aside for projects to
achieve future flood damage reduction.

Connection to the Disaster
Hurricane Irene washed out bridges on Lake Road,
Glenwood Road, Tuthill Road, and State Route 208,
along with a small bridge on Merriewold Lane, severely
hampering the ability of emergency service personnel
to reach residents for rescue. The storm also severely
damaged access roads in and out of residential
subdivisions, leaving people stranded in their homes in
many residential areas, especially in the Village of South
Blooming Grove. Repetitive flooding from past storm
events, including Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm
Lee, is arguably the largest challenge facing the Town
for establishing long-term community health and safety.
This project responds directly to impacts from the most
recent disaster by working towards identifying longterm solutions to areas of chronic flood risk to ensure

public safety and create confidence in the future
of the Town.

Description of Project
The study consists of a multi-pronged approach,
including more detailed hydraulic and hydrologic
modeling and an evaluation of flood mitigation and
community resiliency opportunities. The Town of
Blooming Grove NYRCR Community (Community)
will need all of this information to achieve its goal of
charting a long-term strategy.
This analysis would provide a platform for the Town
to implement final recommendations from the study,
and the Community would set funds aside for projects
to complete future flood damage reduction activities
recommended by the study.
Projects identified by the study for implementation
may include, but will not be limited to:
• Identification of specific project area(s);
• Ways to foster additional stakeholder engagement;

transfer to a definitive list of projects recommended for
implementation under future funding sources.

• Rankings in the plan as the most influential and

importance; and
• Proposed timeline and implementation schedules.

within the Community.

The Committee understands that flooding in the Town
of Blooming Grove, the Village of South Blooming
Grove, and the Village of Washingtonville are
interconnected, and the Town should evaluate flood
control measures for the protection of critical facilities
to determine collective potential impact.
In addition, the creation of additional storage areas to
protect NYRCR Plan Area residents would likely require
regional coordination and development. To this end,
the Committee has invited members of the Village of
Washingtonville NYRCR Committee to attend the Town
of Blooming Grove NYRCR Committee Meetings.
The Village of Washingtonville essentially shares the
same hydrologic system as the Town of Blooming Grove.
This project leverages the Village of Washingtonville
NYRCR Proposed Hydraulic Study, and creates a unique
opportunity to cooperatively pool resources and
ideas. The Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Proposed
Project will continue the work started by the Village of
Washingtonville and will carry it forward throughout
the NYRCR Plan Area, to cover all of the Moodna, Perry,
Cromline, and Satterly Creeks. The opportunity for
regional cooperation presented by undertaking this
study saves money and time, along with leading to a
84

PROPOSED PROJECTS

The implementation of all proposed projects resulting
from this study will likely far exceed the initial allotted
amount of funding provided through the NYRCR
Program, and all recommended projects would not be
able to be completed in the timeline set for the NYRCR
Program. With this concern in mind, projects will be
ranked in the completed study according to feasibility
and effectiveness.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

better understanding of regional flooding conditions,
which do not respect municipal boundaries.

Cost Estimate
The total cost of the project is estimated to be
$1,500,000. It is understood that the Town has the
ability to draw on consultant and internal resources for
the management of this project. The study is estimated
to cost $500,000. The estimated project implementation
costs total $1,000,000. Please note that administrative
costs are not included in the project cost estimates.
Individual cost components of the hydrologic and
hydraulic study include:
• Identification of flood storage
opportunities - $50,000
• Regional flood routing hydrologic and hydraulic
study - $225,000
• Development of a comprehensive lake
management plan - $100,000
• Schunemunk Mountain stormwater
analysis - $75,000
• Identification of stream management
improvement measures - $50,000

Project Benefits
The following summarizes the anticipated benefits of
the project for the Town of Blooming Grove and its
neighboring municipalities, including risk and damage
reduction, and economic and social benefits. The
project’s primary benefit focuses on the usefulness
of the analytical tools created through the study and
the potential of these tools to have a positive regional
impact and to facilitate the implementation of future
flood control measures.

FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
The project will benefit the entire population of the
Town of Blooming Grove (18,028 persons, as of the
85

2010 U.S. Census), by providing recommendations for
projects that increase long-term resiliency and decrease
the potential negative impact of flooding to local
residents, roadways, and businesses.
Upon the study’s completion, one of the more
immediate and notable effects will be a directed
spending effort on projects that will have discernible
impacts to reduce flood damages. Such projects may
decrease the number of road closures due to flooding.
Maintaining road service is important to the community
for many reasons, including the following:
• It allows service vehicles to access all parts
of the Town and to monitor culverts and
bridges for blockages;
• It allows for emergency access to be maintained;
• It decreases costs necessary to rescue and shelter
people during significant storm events;
• It decreases road repair costs, and aids in
preventing potential loss of sewer, water, and
other utilities; and
• After a storm, it reduces trip length of local
residents, as they patronize local restaurants,
shops, and other service providers.
• Additionally, residential properties may benefit
from projects that reduce flood water surface
elevations, especially in areas that have chronic
and widespread flooding damage caused by choke
points or inefficient management of dams.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
As this project calls for a technical analysis of a defined
problem, the exact results and ultimate recommended
actions are unknown at this time. The current level of
investigation undertaken by the NYRCR planning process
has effectively painted a broad picture of the needs,
vulnerabilities, and opportunities for improvement in
the Town of Blooming Grove.
It is recommended that the Town consider a
comprehensive technical analysis to acquire detailed
recommendations for short-term implementable
projects and long-term measures. This would be
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

The advancement of this study will also
ensure that cooperative efforts are
maximized and that risks to critical assets
are minimized across the entire Community.
The development of a lake management plan and
assessment of riparian areas will also establish a more
frequent and standardized monitoring protocol to
ensure natural and hardened infrastructure throughout
the Town remains in good condition. This would go far
to decrease the likelihood of catastrophic failure.

ENVIRONMENTAL
This study and the resulting lake management plan
can help protect the Moodna Creek watershed from
damages caused by high flows, and can produce better
environmental flows during dry periods to support
aquatic habitat. Practices developed based on a detailed
study of the Moodna Basin can lead to greater diversity
and higher quality local habitat, as well as improve
water quality in the NYRCR Plan Area and further
downstream.
The Town could use this information to identify those
places along the floodplain, water courses, and drainage
areas that need the most protection from inappropriate
modification. This would help to maintain high quality of
water and riverine habitat.

ECONOMIC

SOCIAL

The project indirectly supports economic development
by identifying places where commercial investments
would be protected from flooding. Protecting the
Community’s commercial and residential assets is key
to long-term resiliency. It allows the community to get
back to business and work with less damage and fewer
days of lost productivity.

Providing a better distribution of flows in the NYRCR
Plan Area’s streams, and targeting restoration of
degraded riparian areas, can also improve the
classification of Perry, Satterly, and Moodna Creeks
from classification C, i.e., waters supporting fisheries
and suitable for non-contact activities, to a higher
classification that could result in a usage for swimming
and other forms of recreation.

The model developed by this study would also create
a basis for guiding future development and assessing
the impact of development proposals that were not
anticipated by the study. This would allow the Town
of Blooming Grove to be able to approve or request
changes in projects based on a science-driven process,
especially since development impacts are cumulative
over a longer period of time and are more difficult
to assess.
The $1 million set aside for implementation of plan
recommendations would create approximately 7.1
construction jobs, potentially creating employment for
local and regional contractors in addition to 8.4 new
support jobs from increased materials and equipment
sales for suppliers and support industries.

Overwhelmingly, the public supports the improvement
of infrastructure, which is addressed by this project.
Because the project will identify strategies to reduce
flooding, it affects all other categories as well, whether
directly or indirectly.
In addition, according to comments received at
the second Public Engagement Event, the public
overwhelmingly favored the creation of flood storage
areas as a way to alleviate the impact of floodwaters
throughout the Town. The Committee also expressed

ADDITIONAL BENEFITS
The additional benefits that would be created by this
project include the following:
• Regional Coordination and Collaboration: The
project takes advantage of an opportunity to
collaborate with the Village of Washingtonville, a
community that lies within the Town of Blooming
Grove’s municipal boundaries. The Village of
Washingtonville participated in Round 1 of the NY
Rising Program, and has identified a similar study
as a Proposed Project. This proposed expansion
to areas not studied by Washingtonville would
provide a more complete picture of the impact of
flooding across the NYRCR Plan Area, and would
benefit all regional residents.
• Easy Access to Critical Information: The project
would more fully develop available information
into a useful tool that the municipalities in the
Town of Blooming Grove could utilize when
assessing proposed projects in the floodplain. In
addition, the information contained in the study
would help to garner public support for necessary
change that would lead to ecologically sound
decisions and a more resilient community.
• Provides Springboard for Future Resiliency
Projects: By implementing the program identified
in this project, the Town will be better situated
to evaluate and determine where necessary
infrastructure upgrades and flood control/
resiliency improvements are needed. This study
will help to prepare grant applications and
obtain necessary data to implement the future
resiliency projects.

Furthermore, this project would address all
five of the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan’s resiliency strategies:
• Strategy 1: Reduce the impact of riverine flooding
87

on the built environment, including critical
facilities, infrastructure, businesses, and housing.
The study will identify projects that would provide
the most protection to the Community and will
have the greatest impact on flooding of the built
environment. One or more of these recommended
projects will be implemented as the second phase
of this NYRCR project.
• Strategy 2: Improve stormwater management and
drainage systems throughout Town to decrease
risk for homes, businesses, and residents.
A part of this project is related to the impacts
of sheet flow and identifies locations where
stormwater runoff becomes problematic,
especially when these areas run into the
water channels.
• Strategy 3: Improve upon existing capabilities for
emergency preparedness, response, sheltering,
and communication.
The implementation of the study’s
recommendation would aid in the protection of
roads necessary to maintain critical infrastructure
crossing the waterways. It also would allow
residents to escape flooding, if necessary, and have
access to emergency services.
• Strategy 4: Preserve, protect, and enhance
the Town’s natural, recreational, and
cultural resources, and restore natural
floodplain capabilities.
The project will create strategies to assist the
Community in protecting water resources in the
Town by identifying areas requiring protection
from inappropriate development and long-term
stewardship for the benefit of all residents
and wildlife.
• Strategy 5: Assist town residents, officials, and
business owners in becoming better protected
from future flood damages through education,
programming, outreach, and policy initiatives.
Use of the study in future planning and evaluation
would better prepare officials to shape municipal
policy, e.g., comprehensive plans, local ordinances,
and zoning. It would also garner the support
for expenditures and changes needed to better
protect against future flooding events and to

IMPLEMENTATION TIME FRAME

This project seeks to promote one of the core tenants
of successful disaster response—ensuring emergency
responders can reach the areas where help is needed.

The study phase of this project can be completed
within 6-8 months. The implementation of the
proposed projects could take up to 2 years.

The $1.5 million maximum cost of this project seems
relatively low, when compared to the value of the lives
and property that might be saved. This can happen
by ensuring that an ambulance can reach the resident
in cardiac arrest, or that the fire department can
reach people trapped in their homes because of
swiftly-rising floodwaters.

during project implementation. That temporary

Project Background

In the Village of South Blooming Grove, the stormwaterinfused Satterly Creek wreaked havoc on roadways and
bridges. It inundated water resources infrastructure,
damaging many important structures, including the
Village of South Blooming Grove’s Sewer Pump Station.
The tributary to the Satterly Creek, running adjacent to
the South Blooming Grove Firehouse, eroded its bank to
encroach on the foundation of the firehouse and truck
bays, also penetrating the front door of the Village of
South Blooming Grove’s Sewer Pump Station.

Critical facilities and infrastructure in the
Town of Blooming Grove provide vital services to
residents on a day-to-day basis. They also serve as
crucial components of emergency preparedness,
response, and hazard mitigation.

Connection to the Disaster
During Hurricane Irene, a significant amount of runoff
surged through small and large tributaries of the
Satterly, Cromline, Moodna, and Perry Creeks, at times
overtopping spillways, eroding streambanks, and
causing overland flooding.
As floodwaters from Satterly Creek inundated the
basements and first floors of residences on Barnes
Road, the Tappan Water and Waste Water Treatment

The same tributary met with a significant amount of
runoff further downstream, flowing to the Merriewold
Lake and through its spillway. The flow from the
spillway discharged to a channel that makes a relatively
hard turn before being directed back to the original
stream channel of Satterly Creek. A significant amount
of erosion resulted in the discharge of the draining
channel, particularly along the turn before the flow
reached the original streambed.

Current site and building for the Town Maintenance Garage. Photo is

Further downstream, the Town of Blooming Grove
Central Garage and the Salisbury Mills Fire Company
headquarters flooded from rising waters in the Moodna
Creek, inhibiting use of those facilities and damaging
critical equipment housed there. Stormwater entered
through truck bays and backed up through floor drains
at the Salisbury Mills Fire Company headquarters,
partially submerging the back-up generator and
compromising the company’s ability to provide
emergency services.

Description of Project
This project aims to strengthen key critical facilities that
experienced service interruptions due to flooding during
Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm Lee. The project
may be informed by the Town’s new detailed inspection
program and investment prioritization protocol, if
implemented under the NYRCR Plan. The Town could
evaluate the following projects for implementation:
1. F lood mitigation measures for Tappan Waste Water
Treatment Plant
The associated Tappan Water Treatment Facility
on Barnes Road has been elevated to reduce
vulnerability, but the Sewer Treatment Facility
remains vulnerable to flood damage from the Satterly
Creek. This option seeks to install concrete floodwalls
to flood height and install barriers at flood gates.

Proposed relocation site for the Town of Blooming Grove Central
Garage, at 2741 State Route 94 in the Town of Blooming Grove. Photo
is courtesy of NYRCR Mapping Portal.

2. Relocation of Town of Blooming Grove
Central Garage
The Town of Blooming Grove Central Garage is
currently located in the Village of Washingtonville
at 53 West Main Street, roughly 500 feet from the
northern bank of Moodna Creek. The facility includes
three bays and one bathroom, and it is used by
three Town employees for equipment storage,
maintenance activities for the local fleet, and limited
administrative needs.
The garage has flooded repeatedly. Town officials
have recommended to the Committee that the garage
relocate to the Town Highway Garage, at 2741 State
Route 94, which is out of the floodplain. Doing so will
minimize the future flood vulnerability of this critical
facility, and operations at the relocated facility could
benefit from the Proposed Project to provide back-up
power at the Town Highway Garage.
3. Salisbury Mills Fire Company Headquarters
Stormwater Retrofit
Rising waters of the Moodna Creek brought up to 18
inches of water to the Salisbury Mills Fire Company
headquarters, at 2128 State Route 94, inundating the
building through the walls and bay doors and backing
up through the stormwater pipes underfoot.
This project option proposes limited mitigation
measures to protect the mechanical aparatus and

90

PROPOSED PROJECTS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

operational capacity of the Firehouse, including
revising the Sanitary and Domestic Water System
in accordance with Chapter7, American Society of
Civil Engineers (ASCE) 24, to minimize or eliminate
infiltration of floodwaters into the facility and
discharge from the facility into floodwaters, elevating
the mechanical systems, water heaters, air handlers
as well as the automatic transfer switch and back-up
generator above the flood level.
4. Flood mitigation measures for Village of South
Blooming Grove Sewer Pump Station
Flood waters from the Satterly Creek during Hurricane
Irene permeated the Sewer Pump Station at 117
Duelk Ave. in the Village of South Blooming Grove.
Mitigation measures include the installation of rubber
gasket flood panels to seal the vent and door from
future floods.

6. Merriewold Lake Outlet Channel Bank Restoration
During Hurricane Irene, a significant amount of
runoff flowed through Merriewold Lake and over the
spillway. The flow from the spillway was discharged
to a channel that makes a hard turn before directing
back to the original stream channel of Satterly Creek.
A significant amount of erosion occurred during
the discharge of the draining channel, particularly
along the turn before the flow reached the original
streambed. This project would include streambank
stabilization of approximately 250 feet along the
discharge channel of Merriewold Lake.

Project Benefits
The following summarizes the anticipated benefits of
the project based upon the historic losses.

FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
Removing the Town of Blooming Grove Central Garage,
protecting the Salisbury Mills Fire Department, and
protecting South Blooming Grove Firehouse, will ensure
that public works and firefighting crews can continue to
serve the Plan Area’s residents and businesses during
and after an emergency, rather than needing to
redirect resources and time to address damages to
their own facilities.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
This project reduces risk to residents and visitors by
ensuring that critical response agencies, including the
Town Department of Public Works and the Salisbury
Mills Fire Department, can continue to provide service
during a flood event, rather than spend their resources
on protecting their own facilities and equipment. It also
reduces risk to the Town’s wastewater facilities, which
serve the general public and Vulnerable Populations,
ensuring that they can continue to operate during and
after a disaster.

ECONOMIC
This project is based on strengthening local
infrastructure to make the Town more resilient to future
disasters. Investing in flood-proofing and elevation
measures for critical infrastructure demonstrates a
commitment to the future of the Town, its residents,
and its work force. This investment creates greater
confidence for private investors.
In addition, the physical work involved in relocating the
Town of Blooming Grove Central Garage; retrofitting the
Salisbury Mills Fire Department, Tappan Waste Water
Treatment Plant, and Village of South Blooming Grove
Sewer Pump Station; and stabilizing the streambank
that runs near the South Blooming Grove Firehouse will
require service providers to perform the work. These

95

service providers may be local companies. The service
providers’ increased revenue will result in increased
wages being paid to local employees. The money from
these wages will be spent on other local goods and
services, potentially creating additional jobs, increasing
expendable income, and increasing tax revenue.
This work will create approximately 8.8 full-time
equivalent (FTE) construction and 10.4 FTE support jobs
from labor, materials, equipment, and other supply
and support industries. The FTE construction jobs were
estimated based on a methodology developed by the
United States Department of Commerce Economics and
Statistics Administration as presented in the September
2013 Economic Impact of Hurricane Sandy: Potential
Economic Activity Lost and Gained in New Jersey and
New York. This study estimated job creation from
recovery spending on infrastructure projects in New
York and reported 7.15 construction jobs and 8.4 total
jobs per $1,000,000 in construction spending. These
numbers were adjusted to the $1,250,000 construction
estimates for the different aspects of this project.

ENVIRONMENTAL
Each component of this project has environmental
benefits.
• Flood mitigation measures for the Tappan Waste
Water Treatment Plant will prevent floodwater
from inundating the facility and sending untreated
wastewater into the natural environment.
• Ensuring continued operation of the Tappan Waste
Water Treatment Plant and the Village of South
Blooming Grove’s Sewer Pump Station reduces
the likelihood that untreated wastewater will be
released from the facilities, contaminating the
floodwaters and the local environment.
• Relocating the Central Garage out of the floodplain
can help reduce the amount of pollutants in
floodwaters. This type of facility houses hazardous
materials (e.g., gasoline and engine oil) that could
contaminate floodwaters if compromised. In
addition, much of the maintenance equipment
likely has an oily film on it, which would also
pollute the water when the garage floods.
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

• Stabilizing the streambank that runs past the
Firehouse and the Merriewold Lake outlet
channel bank will repair damage to the natural
environment caused by increased stormwater
runoff due to local development.
Furthermore, the facilities identified in this project may
also require renovations to implement the different
portions of this project. These renovations may include
health and safety improvements which could increase
the overall environmental quality within the buildings.

SOCIAL
The project benefits society as a whole in the Town
by ensuring that critical facilities, such as sewer
infrastructure, can continue to operate during and
after a disaster. By implementing these improvements,
the Town is ensuring basic community hygiene and
protecting the health of its residents and visitors.

PUBLIC SUPPORT
At the first Public Engagement Event of this planning
process, held on July 30, 2014, attendees cast their
ballots for projects aligned with each of the six recovery
support functions (RSF). The Infrastructure RSF received
104 (42.3%) out of the 246 votes cast, demonstrating
clear public support for projects that protect critical
infrastructure and services.

two facilities may have an impact on the hydrologic,
hydraulic, and stormwater analyses being conducted
along the Moodna Creek, Satterly Creek, and
Perry Creek.

Project Cost-Benefit Analysis
The benefits of fire department personnel being able
to respond where needed during a flood event, rather
than rededicating time and work to save the equipment
in the firehouse easily outweigh the costs of protecting
the Firehouse. Fire department personnel will be able to
significantly increase the number of lives and property
saved by this measure. The cost of the pump station
mitigation measures is likewise low when compared
to the benefit of an operational sewer system and an
environment free of contamination. The public will
see little impact on their daily lives, as the project is
facility-specific.

IMPLEMENTATION TIME FRAME
This project could be implemented within 1.5 years.

Completion of this project will require Article 15
Protection of Waters approvals from the New York
State Department of Environmental Conservation (NYS
DEC), as well as the Town of Blooming Grove or Village
of South Blooming Grove, depending on the individual
project component.

rests with the Town of Blooming Grove or the Village of

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Many changes to the drainage patterns have occurred
over the years as private owners altered culverts
and open swales on individual properties, with little
understanding of how these changes can
exacerbate flooding.

Project Background

As a result, the original open trenches
built to divert stormwater runoff to nearby
brooks have significantly reduced capacity
and are often overwhelmed during storms.

Mountain Lodge Park was originally built on the western
slope of Schunemunk Mountain as summer home
residences with natural drainage systems of mostly
grass-lined swales and culverts, incorporated alongside
private gravel roads referred to as “trails” throughout
the development. The community splits into two
distinct sides—the Mountain Lodge Park side and the
Glenwood Hills side.
Occupancy on both sides has changed over the years
from part-time summer vacationers to full-time
residents, most of which receive only low to moderate
income. Approximately 800 homes exist in this area.

Brooks and other streams have also been affected by
the excess runoff in past storms, such as occurred in
Oakwood Trail North, where runoff washed away the
entire culvert structure and severely eroded trenches
(as visualized by the photographs on this page and
the next). During Hurricane Irene in 2011, many of the
roads and trails became impassible and contained large
debris, including trees, which became undermined by
fast-moving water draining through trails and trenches.

Featured here is the location of the proposed Mountain Lodge
drainage improvements and flood damage reduction measures.
Location map courtesy of SCAPE / Landscape Architecture PLLC.

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

98

PROPOSED PROJECTS

Mountain Lodge Drainage
Improvements and Flood
Damage Reduction

PROPOSED PROJECTS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Trench and culvert used to divert runoff to a nearby brook at Oakwood
Trail North. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

The trench extends between properties at Oakwood Trail North. Photo
is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

Connection to the Disaster
In 2011, the Mountain Lodge Park community received
significant damage to its infrastructure. Since many of
the roads are gravel and drainage is poorly managed,
roads experienced significant flooding, leading to
washouts and inaccessibility by emergency personnel.
Some of the trails also contained large debris (e.g., rocks
and felled trees), carried in swift-moving streams that
overwhelmed Perry Creek.
Because of the limited ability of the residents to recover
from the storm, many of the losses to the stormwater
infrastructure remain in disrepair and threaten other
areas downstream. Woodcock Mountain Road,
which drains uncontrolled into the mobile home park
downstream and threatens residential dwellings, serves
as a prime example. In addition, several homes in the
Mountain Lodge Park community lie along

Description of Project
This project could be implemented in two phases.
The first part would consist of providing a series of
standard plans and detailed drawings for repairing and/
or upgrading existing degraded stormwater ditches.
This portion of the project could be implemented by
the home owners directly or the Mountain Lodge
Park Glenwood Hills Residential Association
(Homeowners Association).

This section of the project also includes a set of standard
plans designed for implementation by individual owners
to replace the historic drainage swales and improve
overland flow conditions. Plans would detail either the
re-establishment of the existing vegetated swales or a
piped alternative, if the current land use does not allow
for an open swale. Additional standard plans would
be made available to address road crossings over the
major drainages within the Mountain Lodge Park and
Glenwood Hills communities, primarily along Perry
Creek and its tributary. The Community would use these
standard plans proactively to improve flow routing and
accessibility during large storm events.
The second portion of the project focuses on outreach
and education to Mountain Lodge Park homeowners
through the Homeowners Association to assist in the
implementation of the plans. Part of the outreach could

Project Benefits
FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE

The project helps to prevent loss of life
by creating a safer system of roadway
access for emergency personnel and
residents during evacuation, assuming the
implementation and ongoing maintenance
of the drainage system improvements.
The project would also help maintain emergency
access of the Mountain Lodge Firehouse, located in
the community.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
A visual inspection of the area shows a need for
additional drainage swales to capture flows and direct
them safely around private property. Widespread re101

establishment of drainage swales on the uphill side of
properties could significantly reduce localized flooding
problems. Establishment of more robust stream
crossings would also reduce the likelihood of culverts
experiencing total failure and would have a significant
impact on maintaining access to homeowners over the
main drainage channels within the community.
The implementation of this project would reduce the risk
of damage to homes occupied by the community’s low
and moderate income residents. The implementation
of the project would provide access to the residents in
case of emergency, protect utility services, and allow
more time for residents to evacuate during significant
storm events.

ECONOMIC
The protection and preservation of the Mountain Lodge
housing fills an essential need for the low and moderate
income residents in this community. Low to moderate
income families include a variety of householders,
ranging from young families with children to single,
educated adults starting their first jobs or single adults
who want to live close to established parents to retirees
on fixed incomes. If these homes are lost, lower and
moderate income residents have few options if they
want to continue to live in the Town of Blooming Grove
and surrounding areas. Affordable housing has been
identified as a priority need in Orange County, especially
in southern Orange County in the Towns of Blooming
Grove, Monroe, Goshen, Woodbury, and Chester.

Preservation of affordable housing
benefits the local economy, because it
ensures nearby housing for those who
are more likely to be employed in the
growing service or retail sectors, such as
Woodbury Commons.
Without affordable housing, costs to work in Orange
County at an entry-level position become more
expensive and can lead to slower economic growth.
Projects that increase the resiliency of these homes
benefit not just the residents, but also local businesses
and the broader community by helping residents thrive.

ADDITIONAL BENEFITS

The implementation of this project will help reduce
erosion of roads and trails, protecting the tree cover and
reducing materials that eventually are deposited into
streams and creeks. This reduction of soil, rocks, and
debris helps to protect water quality and wildlife habitat
in the stream and forests surrounding Mountain Lodge
homes. It also helps to preserve the capacity of the
existing floodplain.

Although the buildings in converted summer housing
communities tend to be small and the homes of low
to moderate income residents are often disregarded,
Mountain Lodge Park provides a significant link with the
development history for most of the communities in
the Plan Area. It also serves as an important source for
understanding the history of the development in Orange
County, as well as the residents that came from New
York City to summer in the Hudson Valley.

SOCIAL
The Homeowners Association may play a significant role
in the homeowner outreach and education component
of this project. Regular Homeowners Association
meetings may be used to provide homeowners with
increased access to resources, and encourage overall
involvement in actions to reduce flood-damage
vulnerability on a community-wide scale.

Likewise, this project would protect
two communities with a strong sense
of community identity and history.
PUBLIC SUPPORT

general public was provided with the opportunity to
“spend” virtual money on projects they felt were the
