in Infrastructure, which is addressed by this project. In
addition, the project addresses Community Capacity
Building (the second strongest category) and Housing
(the third strongest category).
Although no official source has documented the
historical status of these homes, the project helps
to protect the cultural resources associated with
the history of the development of this community.
Additionally, 11 percent of the vote supported
Vulnerable Populations, which includes the low
and moderate income homes predominating in
this area. Finally, the project is supported by the
Homeowners Association, one of the primary
beneficiaries of this project.

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Project Background
The Town of Blooming Grove currently has an
enforceable zoning code that includes some protection
for its 100-year floodplain, streams, wetlands, and other
water surfaces and resources that help to control and
absorb the water during flooding events. Despite this,
the Committee identified the need to further refine the
zoning code to better protect flood resiliency resources
and ensure that new development is built in areas
safe from flooding, as well as begin to address existing
homes vulnerable to flooding.

Connection to the Disaster
Flooding and stormwater flows from Hurricane Irene
and Tropical Storm Lee severely damaged residential

and commercial areas in 2011. These areas included
housing that serves the needs of Vulnerable Populations
in the Plan Area and businesses that play a vital role in
the economic recovery of the Town.
Zoning codes have ongoing impacts to the Town. These
impacts have the potential to be profound, depending
on the size of the proposed development and the level
of practical application necessary.

These locally adopted regulations also
have far-reaching effects on the
Community, because changes to the
built environment are considered to be
permanent investments.
When the subdivision on Barnes Road was built in
the 1980s, the Town could not anticipate the impact of
stronger and more frequent storm events. Additionally, the
Town did not closely examine the cumulative impact of all
of the development along the Moodna Basin because no
perceived need to do so had been identified.

Source: Tetra Tech, Inc.

only as new information becomes available. Therefore,
they often only partially address the need for protection
of the natural environment as an asset used in the
protection of property and life. This gap creates the
need for this proposed project, i.e., to ensure that
future building in the Town of Blooming Grove leads to
safe housing for Vulnerable Populations and compels
building owners and developers to incorporate flood
mitigation practices to housing in floodprone areas.

Description of Project

Shown here is the cover page of the latest [Town of Blooming Grove
Comprehensive Plan], completed in 2005. Courtesy of the Town of
Blooming Grove.

Development often strips natural resources that provide
protection from storms, such as tree cover, reducing
capacity in acceptable limits, as defined by the codes
and regulations enforceable at the time of the approvals
and during the subsequent construction of the homes.

All projects, regardless of their date of
construction, reflect the best knowledge
and practices available at the time to
protect people, property, and infrastructure.
The original drainage infrastructure for the Barnes
Road homes matched the construction and design
requirements of the time, and construction of the
homes proceeded in accordance with that knowledge.
In recent years, however, science has recognized
the importance of floodplain and channel capacity
preservation. New construction standards allow these
natural features to exist with as little modification as
possible, instead of allowing development in areas
where repetitive flooding events could be likely.
The implementation of this broader vision coincides
with the protection of water quality, lives, and property.
Locally adopted zoning codes often change slowly and
105

This project would include a comprehensive review of
current Plan Area zoning and subdivision regulations.
This review would identify where the regulations could
be improved to enhance the community’s resiliency to
future storm events. Potential updates would include
the following:
• Enhance the current water surface protection
(i.e., Flood Damage Prevention Ordinance) to
reflect standards recommended by the Moodna
Creek Study and to provide a system of evaluating
current structures in the 100-year floodway. The
Town could utilize the evaluation system if they
receive a request for expansion of a building’s
footprint or a building becomes damaged in future
flooding events and requires rehabilitation.
• Review the existing zoning map to ensure that
higher density zoning opportunities are located
in areas well outside floodway and floodplain.
The Committee hopes to create opportunities for
new business to take advantage of planned and
potential recreational pathways and to create
affordable housing in desirable mixed-use areas.
• Review the list of uses permitted in zoning districts
to determine their appropriateness in all zones,
especially when planned adjacent to flood-prone
areas or when construction may adversely affect
the water quality during a significant storm event.
• Further refine the Rural Cross Roads Development
districts (RC-1 and RC-2) to attract redevelopment
of these centers to fulfill the original concepts
expressed by the code and to ensure that zoning
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

• Review and create a limit of impervious surfaces
for each zoning district. The code currently
regulates building sizes; however, the code does
not address the impacts from other permanent
structures that can have detrimental impacts on
individual site stormwater absorption.
• Encourage the use of green infrastructure
on individual lots and develop a method to
ensure maintenance.
• Consider the use of a “form-based” zoning
code. Form-based zoning codes require the
development of a conservation plan prior
to the proposed layout. These plans identify
significant natural features of the site that often
align with the protection of the site’s ability to
absorb floodwaters and stormwater runoff. The
residential district (RR, as written in the current
code) requires some of these elements at this
date; however, it excludes significant tree cover
from evaluation. This evaluation should occur in
all zoning districts, especially in locations with
planned housing development. This approach has
two main benefits: (1) protection of floodways and
the natural environment; and (2) retention of the
natural beauty of the area, which enhances the
quality of life within the Community.
• Consider adopting an ordinance to protect and
transfer development rights from farmland to
areas that can accommodate more density.
• Consider adopting protection of larger trees
and forested areas, especially when located on
sloped areas or when providing protection along
streambanks.
• Adopt or update the recreation plan to better
utilize floodplain areas, and create a schedule
of required recreation fees. Recreation fees
can be used to purchase land and enhance the
recreational use of areas within a floodplain.
• Ensure the Town Code has sufficient flexibility to
address and protect all future FEMA-designated
floodplains and those significant environmental
features designated by other state agencies
(e.g., streams rated “C” and above), with special
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

PROPOSED PROJECTS

areas are appropriately identified.

attention to those streams and water resources
important to the ecology and water quality
of the region.

Cost Estimate
The total estimated project cost is $80,000. This
cost includes the update of zoning and subdivision
regulations and allows for the services of an
experienced certified planner and review by the
Town’s civil engineer and Town attorney.
It is anticipated that the project would require review
of the existing Town of Blooming Grove Comprehensive
Plan, revisions to the zoning code and subdivision
regulations, and preparation of a Generic Environmental
Impact Statement (GEIS) in accordance with current
State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA).
An environmental impact statement examines the
impacts of full build-out of the zoning code, i.e., if all
property were to be developed under the new code,
and compares this impact with existing codes. The
cost estimate does not include those changes to the
comprehensive plan that are required to implement
zoning changes.
In addition, the State of New York requires that
Towns adopt a recreation plan to justify and collect
any recreation fees. The creation of a recreation plan
would allow the Town to develop a broader vision of
its recreational opportunities and needs. If a recreation
plan has already been adopted, the plan should be
modified to include recreation fees. The cost to create
or modify a recreational plan was not considered as
part of the total estimated project; however, this
aspect would cost an estimate of $40,000 if made a
project add-on. The development of a recreational plan
could require up to two years for full completion, and
it would also require consideration under SEQRA, most
likely with a GEIS.
It is understood that the Town of Blooming Grove has
the ability to draw on consultant and internal resources
for the management of this project.

PROPOSED PROJECTS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Project Benefits
Development of ordinance improvements has several
long-range and permanent impacts that would be
realized over time and as development proposals
would be implemented. The following sections explain
these impacts in more detail. Most notably, policy
changes lead to better communities in the long run,
and communities often view these changes as part of a
broader vision to enhance, protect, and create value.

FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
Changing the zoning to protect natural resources and
removing development from flood-prone areas will
benefit the entire Plan Area by preserving its natural
beauty and protective functionality.
These changes would eventually decrease the number
of residents in unsafe and flood-prone locations,
reducing the need for emergency sheltering/rescue and
adding to the overall quality of life for residents with of
mixed-use centers that encourage community building.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
The exact reduction of risk is unknown, since this project
relates to a change in policy that would be realized over
several years and as projects are implemented. Risks,
however, would be reduced in the following areas:
• Remove future residential and business
development from flood-prone areas and protect
property investment;
• Stabilize and enhance the tax base in the Plan Area
as businesses grow uninterrupted by significant
storm events;
• Reduce the impacts of development on water
quality and other ecological systems in Town;
• Reduce the impact of loss of character and open
space;
• Decrease the potential for stormwater runoff from
developed properties;
• Encourage housing development in areas where
higher density is feasible, and reduce the need for
107

evacuation and sheltering;
• Protect business investments from flooding, and
reduce lost business income and productivity;
• Address short-term and long-term risks related to
development of the Community’s resources;
• Reduce the need for flood insurance in the
Community; and
• Reduce the need for emergency rescue during
storm events.

ECONOMIC

Mixed-use centers established in areas that
can receive more density typically spur
economic development through synergies
between affordable housing and small
business start-ups.
These mixed-use centers are designed to complement
one another and facilitate a sense of identity in the
Community. Furthermore, when flooding does not
threaten development, the Community realizes
a significant cost-savings benefit, and so do local
residents, the government, and emergency service
providers. This increases the Community’s overall
resiliency and helps it to recover faster from flooding or
other hazard events. It also allows for opportunities to
develop shared public services (i.e., water, sewer, and
transportation) at a reasonable cost to the development
community, which passes along to the consumer.
A safe development environment also provides a link
to stability in terms of property values and continued
opportunities for investment. In addition, by identifying
appropriate new businesses areas that enhance and
protect the investor, the community provides potential
permanent access to goods and services in more easily
accessible locations. Vulnerable residents may need to
rely on easy access to goods and services that are within
walking distance of their homes, particularly residents
without consistent access to transportation, whether for
physical or financial causes.
The Town could place community economic centers
with a mixed-use element near locations that have
future potential for alternative transportation benefits,
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

in the floodplain benefit the entire Plan Area.
Zoning changes that protect residents from flooding
further reduces the demand for emergency
sheltering and rescue.

Newer trends in development suggest that people want
to live near protected open spaces, even at the expense
of personally maintained space.

Further, this project would address three of the Town of
Blooming Grove NYRCR project’s resiliency strategies:

When developers respond to the demand
for protected open space, practical and costsaving benefits to the Town emerge in terms
of dedicated infrastructure maintenance.
ENVIRONMENTAL
The maintenance of wetlands, streams, floodways,
floodplains, and trees would protect flora and fauna
from further degradation and would maintain
water quality and ecological diversity in the Town
of Blooming Grove.

SOCIAL
Providing protective measures to maintain open spaces
and places related to the natural forests also enhances
the quality of life enjoyed by residents. The project
would also benefit Vulnerable Populations by identifying
buildable housing sites that are outside of floodplain
areas and close to jobs, goods, and services.

Strategy 1: Reduce the impact of riverine flooding
on the built environment, including critical facilities,
infrastructure, businesses, and housing. The project
would advance the protection of the natural system’s
ability to receive and absorb floodwaters and would
place new critical facilities in areas that do not flood.
Strategy 2: Improve stormwater management and
drainage systems throughout Town to decrease risk for
homes, businesses, and residents. This project directly
relates to the fulfillment of this strategy.
Strategy 4: Preserve, protect, and enhance the
Town’s natural, recreational, and cultural resources,
and restore natural floodplain capabilities. The project
would create a policy that would protect natural
floodplain capabilities and would encourage the
removal of buildings and structures to areas outside
the natural floodplain.

votes, or 42.3%).

For an $80,000 investment, the Town would be able
to better ensure that its regulations protect millions of
dollars in structures and property throughout the Town,
not just in the floodplain. The financial benefits far
exceed the financial costs of this project.

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Project Background
Critical facilities and infrastructure in the Town of
Blooming Grove provide vital services to residents on
a day-to-day basis; however, they also serve as crucial
components of emergency preparedness, response,
and hazard mitigation.
During Superstorm Sandy, high winds downed trees
and power lines, causing widespread power outages in
the Plan Area, including at critical infrastructure.
Similar power outages have occurred numerous
times in the Town’s history, including from seasonal
thunderstorms and not just severe storms, i.e., storms
similar to Hurricane Irene, Tropical Storm Lee, and
Superstorm Sandy.

Description of Project
This project aims to obtain and install permanent backup power sources for critical facilities and infrastructure
in the Town not addressed in other proposed projects.
This project would include an evaluation of existing
critical facilities’ needs for redundant power generation.
The Community will consider use of solar or green
energy, where feasible.

South Blooming Grove Village Hall currently does not have back-up
power. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Priority locations include the following:
• South Blooming Grove Village Hall; and
• Town Highway Garage at 2741 State Route 94
(also the proposed site for relocation of the new/
relocated Town of Blooming Grove Central Garage.
If the Central Garage relocates, the proposed
back-up power source will benefit multiple
critical facilities).

Cost Estimate
The estimated project cost for the project, which
includes installation of the generators and the electrical
work to connect them to the facilities, is $83,320.

Project Benefits
The following summarizes the anticipated benefits of
the project based upon the historic losses.

FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
This project will have substantial benefits to the Town.
Coordination of the Plan Area’s responders requires that
the Emergency Operations Center (EOC) be operational
throughout the preparedness, response, and recovery
phases of a hazard event. Also, public works activities,
such as debris removal and closing unsafe roadways,
must continue during and after a storm. This project
will help prevent injuries and deaths among the Town’s
population by ensuring that these critical services can
be performed.

This entrance and driveway of the Town Highway Garage at 2741
State Route 94, is recommended as a location for installing back-up
emergency power. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
This project reduces risk to residents and visitors by
ensuring that critical infrastructure can continue to
operate during and after a disaster. It also reduces the
risk of additional damage or increased maintenance
to systems and facilities as a result of a prolonged
power outage.

ECONOMIC
This project focuses on strengthening local
infrastructure to make the Town more resilient to
future disasters. Investing in back-up power generation
capabilities at critical facilities demonstrates a
commitment to the future of the Town, its residents,
and its work force. This investment creates greater
confidence for private investors.
In addition, the physical installation of permanent
generators at critical facilities will require service
providers to perform the work. These service providers
may consist of local companies. The service providers’
increased revenue will result in increased wages
paid to local employees. Employees will then spend
111

the money from these wages on local goods and
services, potentially creating additional jobs, increasing
expendable income, and increasing taxes.
Using natural gas or solar power as fuel for the
generators also has economic benefits. Natural gas
is generally cheaper than gasoline or diesel fuel, and
sunlight is free. Over time, the operation of natural
gas or solar power generators will ultimately save the
Community more money than the operation of gasoline
or diesel generators.
This work will create approximately 0.6 full-time
equivalent (FTE) construction and 0.7 FTE support jobs
from labor, materials, equipment, and other supply and
support industries.

ENVIRONMENTAL
The environmental benefit of this project relates to the
type of fuel used by the generators. Gasoline, diesel
fuel, natural gas, and solar power can all fuel a back-up
power generator. Natural gas generators, as opposed
to gasoline or diesel fuel generators, result in a cleaner
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

In addition, the critical facilities in need of the
generators may require renovations for installation
and operation of the generator. These renovations may
increase the environmental quality in the building due
to requisite health and safety improvements made
during the renovation.

SOCIAL
Residents often use public buildings to hold social
events; the South Blooming Grove Village Hall could
serve a similar function for the local community.
Ensuring that the Village Hall has back-up power would
allow these events to continue if there is a power
outage for any reason.

PUBLIC SUPPORT
At the first Public Engagement Event of this NYRCR
planning process, held on July 30, 2014, attendees cast
their ballots for projects aligned with each of the six
RSFs. The Infrastructure RSF received 104 (42.3%) votes,
out of the 246 votes cast. The next highest vote tally
was the Community Planning and Capacity Building RSF,
which received 46 votes (18.7%). This demonstrates that
the public clearly favors infrastructure projects such as
this one, i.e., ensuring that critical infrastructure can
operate during power outages.

Project Cost-Benefit Analysis
This project’s goals, i.e., ensuring uninterrupted Town
Highway Department emergency operations services
and the effective coordination of all emergency
operations in the Village of South Blooming Grove, carry
the same benefit to the protection of human life and
property as other projects that focus on maintaining
access for emergency responders.
The value of the lives and property protected by
emergency responders outweigh the financial costs of
the project. The Town of Blooming Grove and the Village
of South Blooming Grove will also realize economic
benefit that exceeds the financial cost of the project
when they need to use their generators, because the
two municipalities will save the cost of an emergency
fuel delivery by being connected in advance to the
natural gas supply lines.

IMPLEMENTATION TIME FRAME
This project can be implemented within six months.

Generator hook-ups will need to meet electrical and
all other applicable codes.

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Project Background
The Citizen Corps National Survey (2009) indicates that
29% of Americans have not prepared for a disaster,
because they think that emergency responders will
assist them if needed. Results also indicate that over
60% expect to rely on emergency responders in the
first 72 hours following a disaster. While local, state,
and Federal government agencies will execute their
emergency response functions, communications to the
public need to convey a more realistic understanding
of the emergency response capacity and capability and
need to emphasize the importance of an individual
citizen’s self-reliance. Inaccessibility during storm events
offers a valid threat to the NYRCR Plan Area.

The Town of Blooming Grove and the
Village of South Blooming Grove only have
two main roads. These roads have certain
limitations that will impact the ability of
emergency vehicles to travel, especially if
damage occurs to the roadways.
Under these circumstances, an individual’s protective
measures and stored resources (e.g., moving to higher
ground, boarding up windows, stored food and water,
a household emergency plan, staying informed on
emerging situations) can reduce the risk of damage
and injury.

Connection to the Disaster
During periods of disaster, people may end up isolated
from external assistance. They may also have limited
or no access to normal community and societal
resources and functions for several days. In the Town of
Blooming Grove, several communities have experienced
such isolation, including Mountain Lodge Park, and
Merriewold Lake. During the past disasters of Hurricane
Irene, Tropical Storm Lee, and Superstorm Sandy, each
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

of these communities has presented access challenges
for emergency responders. In the case of Mountain
Lodge Park, poor drainage and steep grades led to
washed out roads. In Merriewold Lake, flooding resulted
in access bridges becoming structurally unsound,
barring response vehicles from entering.

Description of Project

This community preparedness and
outreach program would implement
and disseminate increased emergency
awareness and drive action to better
prepare the NYRCR Plan Area for all-hazards
emergencies and disasters.
The program would strengthen whole community
partnerships around emergency preparedness by
utilizing various approaches to engage the public and
private sectors, families and individuals, those with
functional needs and Vulnerable Populations, and low
to moderate income populations who do not have
reliable access to the Internet. This program reinforces
and supports existing preparedness outreach efforts by
Federal, State, and county government agencies; private
sector businesses; non-profits; and community/faithbased organizations.
The Committee identified the following specific
pieces of information as priorities for information
dissemination:
• Be Informed: Citizens should learn what protective
measure to take before, during, and after an
emergency.
• Blooming Grove Emergency Alert Program: Public
safety officials use reliable systems to alert the
public and local facilities in the event of natural
or human-caused disasters (via reverse 911,
social media, NY-Alert, New York 211, etc.). The
dissemination of timely and accurate information
during a disaster particularly benefits communities
with access limitations. The Town should
disseminate public information through several
methods and not solely rely on modes dependent

on electricity (e.g., social media, text messages,
and TV announcements).
• Public Safety and Preparedness Information:
The Town could publish and make easily accessible

of Blooming Grove citizens. Specific populations
targeted by the materials include:

citizens should have for different situations?

• Make an Emergency Plan: Disasters can strike at
any point. Information should be readily available
to the public to help them prepare an emergency
plan for their home, vehicle, and workplace.
• Build an Emergency Kit: A disaster supplies kit
consists of a collection of basic items a household
may need to be self-reliant for the first 72 hours
post-disaster. A recommended supplies list should
be available to the citizens of Blooming Grove.
• Develop a Business Continuity Plan: Like
individuals, every business should have a plan
to prepare for, respond to, and recover from a
disaster. Information on and/or a template for a
business continuity plan should be made readily
available to businesses operating within
Blooming Grove.
• Get Involved: The formula to ensure a safer and
more prepared Plan Area consists of volunteers,
a trained and informed public, and increased
support of emergency response agencies during
disasters. The Town could develop and share
information on how members of the public can get
involved before a disaster. Some organizations for
involvement include Citizen Corps, the American
Red Cross, and Voluntary Organizations Active in
Disaster (VOAD).

for preparedness education and training?
• Be Prepared: Being prepared for a disaster or
emergency involves educating oneself, making a
plan to act, and testing that plan.

Cost Estimate
The total project cost is estimated at $15,000, which
includes the development, print and dissemination
of all public outreach material; outreach efforts to
register residents for Blooming Grove’s emergency
communication system; and the development of a
website/social media site (e.g., Facebook) to store

reliable, and rapid emergency communications. Please
note that administrative costs are not included in the
project cost estimates.

Project Benefits
FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
Emergency services rely heavily on prepared citizens
to conduct essential services for those most in need.
During emergency situations, this need increases
substantially. This project focuses on encouraging civic
responsibility and promoting more self-reliant residents
during times of disaster. This not only protects individual
households, it strengthens the entire Community.
Having citizens and businesses fully prepared and
self-sufficient for 72 hours post-disaster will increase
the overall public safety and emergency response
capabilities in the Town of Blooming Grove.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
This project takes an all-hazards approach to preparing
the citizens and businesses of Blooming Grove to be
more self-reliant during times of disaster. This project
reduces risk in the following areas:

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

• Personal preparedness for disasters can
significantly mitigate or reduce the impact a
disaster can have on one’s household and
overall community.
• Being prepared can reduce the feelings of fear,
anxiety, and loss that often accompany disasters.
• Individual citizens’ level of preparedness plays a
critical role in supporting disaster response efforts.
Evidence from past disasters suggests friends and
family members, themselves victims of disaster,
provide the most vital rescue work, including the
immediate provision of first aid, transportation
of causalities to hospitals for treatment, and the
provision of food and shelter.
• According to the Insurance Information Institute,
15-40%of businesses fail following a natural or
human-caused disaster. In addition, FEMA states
that 40% of businesses struck by a disaster never
reopen, and of those that do reopen, 25% close
again within two years. Research has shown
that businesses with a continuity plan in place
can drastically reduce a disaster’s impact and
return to normal work operations faster than
those companies who do not invest in effective
preparedness planning.

ECONOMIC
While there are few anticipated direct economic
benefits (i.e., permanent jobs secured or added;
potential increase in economic activity) as a result of
this proposed project, it does offer great potential in
decreasing the impact on already taxed emergency
services budgets by lessening the number of responses
necessary before, during, and after a disaster.
In addition, encouraging the development and
implementation of business continuity plans for those
businesses operating in the Plan Area promotes a
quicker return to normal business operations and
stimulates the economy. Past disasters have proven that
companies which proactively plan for disasters are the
first to return to business.

environment. As a result, increased pro-social behavior,
both at the household and community level, may occur.
In the past, citizen education has also influenced policy
controls on hazardous materials, safe and renewable
energy, recycling, and water conservation.

As stated above, this project focuses on encouraging

self-reliance.

Project Cost-Benefit Analysis
By implementing a system to warn residents of
impending danger, the Town and Village ensure that
residents have enough time to protect their property
and evacuate from hazard areas, whether on their own
or with assistance from the government. Fewer at-risk
residents yield less loss of life or injury in the Plan Area
and less risk to emergency responders on duty during
a disaster. The financial cost of the project is very
low compared to the reduced risk to residents
and responders.

ongoing basis.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Detailed Inspection
Program and Investment
Prioritization Protocol
Project Background
Transportation corridors are typically a major victim of
flooding events. Numerous roadways in and around
the Town of Blooming Grove have been impacted by
the floodwaters from local creeks and tributaries. The
damage includes the potential for complete roadway
collapse, culvert failure, road washouts, and more,
creating systemic issues that not only threaten the
regional transportation system but also the health and
safety of the motorists who need to use the roadways.

Connection to the Disaster
The Committee, with help from Town officials and
past damage reports, has identified multiple roadway
segments which would be evaluated for improvement
through the Detailed Inspection Program and
Investment Prioritization Protocol. Priority locations for
improvements include those that most heavily relied
upon by first responders and other emergency services
personnel, along with roads that currently threaten the
health and safety of motorists in their existing condition.

For instance, the Y intersection of State
Route 208 and Clove Road in the Village
of South Blooming Grove is a dangerous
intersection and one that gets routinely
flooded during severe storms. When
floodwaters overtop this intersection,
emergency response access along the
State Route 208 corridor is inhibited.
Representatives from the Town of Blooming Grove
Planning Board and the Village of South Blooming Grove
Mayor’s office reported that improvements at this Y
intersection will allow full access to emergency vehicles
to residents in the Village of South Blooming Grove on
both State Route 208 and Clove Road. Plans to improve
the entire intersection were designed and approved at
both the State and County levels; however, they were
never fully implemented. While this section of State
Route 208, just south of the gas station at Clove Road,
was repaved within the last decade, a proposed traffic
signal remains to be installed and flood problems persist.
The Committee identified another priority location for
investment as one of the three crossroads in the Town
that connect State Route 208 and State Route 94–Round
Hill Road, Horton Road, and Hudson Road. All three
roads became impassable during Hurricane Irene due to
floodwaters overtopping box culverts and bridges. This
overtopping prohibited emergency responders from
travelling freely between the two main corridors and
population centers in Town.
To improve future emergency operations during flood
events, one or more of these roads must receive
the improvements necessary to guarantee passage
during extreme conditions. This will allow emergency
responders safe passage through the Town of Blooming
Grove and the Village of South Blooming Grove.
These roads and bridges, along with the others listed,
experienced significant damage during Hurricane Irene,
Tropical Storm Lee, and/or Superstorm Sandy. They
have also been identified as having a higher level of
vulnerability to experiencing flood damage.

This is a dangerous intersection at State Route 208 and Clove Road in the
Village of South Blooming Grove. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

Description of Project
This project seeks a holistic, sustainable approach
to increasing transportation system resiliency to
future storms and to addressing repetitive failures on
vulnerable roadway segments throughout the Town of
Blooming Grove. After fully evaluating the components
of the existing transportation network in the Town and
by utilizing the findings of the Committee-proposed
Additional Hydrologic (Flood Routing), Hydraulic,
and Stormwater Analysis project, the Committee
recommends the funding and implementation
of a detailed inspection program and investment
prioritization protocol for Town-owned bridges
and culverts.

This project involves the creation of a
field inspection program to assess the
current condition of the infrastructure,
the conduct of a basic hydraulic analysis to
determine capacity of inspected systems,
and the preparation of a rough cost
estimate for replacement or upgrade.

• State Route 94
• State Route 208, south of Mountain Road near
a culvert that experiences frequent overtopping

A field inspection protocol will standardize data
collection to examine the physical condition of the
crossing, along with the upstream and downstream
area, and will collect necessary information to assess
the available and needed hydraulic capacity of the
structure. This data will be used to make qualitative
assessments on whether replacements or upgrades are
required due to physical condition, flooding concerns,
or both.
A prioritization scheme will establish a list of community
priorities for culvert and bridge improvements located
throughout the Town of Blooming Grove, based on the
quantitative and qualitative assessments.

within the Town and facilitate a faster and better use
of additional funding that might become available after
future disasters.

Project Benefits
FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
The inspection program and prioritization protocol
will benefit the entire population of the Town of
Blooming Grove (18,028 persons, as of the 2010 U.S.
Census) by directing resources towards infrastructure
and transportation projects that increase long-term
resiliency and decrease the potential negative impact
of flooding to local roadways.
Once the inspections are complete, one of the more
immediate and notable effects of the ensuing projects
will be a decrease in the number of road closures due
to flooding. Secondary benefits from a decrease in road
closures include a decrease in the costs necessary to
detour people, decreased temporary repair costs, and
prevention of a potential loss of sewer, water,
and other utilities.
Additionally, once roadway improvements have been
implemented as a result of this project, the overall
safety for emergency response or other essential
personnel who need to be able to access all areas of
the community during a storm event will increase.
This is especially important during events that result in
evacuations, supply distribution, and any other need for
emergency assistance. Socially Vulnerable Populations
may be at greater risk during such severe events and
would also benefit from secured roadways.
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

The prioritization protocol will identify and encourage
the implementation of necessary roadway projects,
such as replacing and upgrading culverts, repairing
roads, installing bridges and other measures that will
lead to less overtopping, sheeting, and other forms of
riverine and overland flooding.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
Existing transportation infrastructure is generally
designed for a variety of risk levels, depending on
ownership, level of use, flood considerations, and
community value. The inspection protocol will quantify
the current level of risk for individual projects and
establish the desired level of risk to determine the
amount of risk reduction that can be obtained
through project improvements. This will help
the community prioritize future investments in
transportation infrastructure.

ECONOMIC

The biggest asset to this project is its cost
effectiveness and ability to efficiently
use limited NYRCR and Town funding to
implement a sustainable record-keeping
tool. This tool could serve a springboard
to identify future resiliency projects
and accurately tracks damages and
vulnerabilities in the Community.
Although the project will have limited direct economic
benefits, it provides numerous indirect benefits. A
detailed, easy-to-use, and accurate record keeping
tool will help the Town make informed decisions
about future resiliency infrastructure and economic
development projects, preventing wasteful and
redundant spending. By regularly assessing critical Town
infrastructure, Town officials can commit money to less
costly maintenance activities rather than more costly
total replacement, extending the useful life of new and
existing infrastructure and reducing capital costs of the
Town in the future.
Many disasters also lead to an increased demand
on staff time, even during the recovery phase. By
implementing this project, staff will more efficiently

ENVIRONMENTAL

In addition, comments from the second Public
Engagement Event included the following
recommendations that are related to or could benefit
from this project:

Inappropriately sized or degrading transportation
infrastructure can result in disconnections of habitat
along riparian corridors. This can prevent movement
of aquatic life or sediment along a river reach, putting
a dynamic stream environment at greater risk of
instability and degradation.
Catastrophic failure of infrastructure located in riparian
corridors can also result in a sudden environmental
shock to a natural system, leading to degradation that
can take years or decades to recover. Regular inspection
and maintenance of hardened infrastructure helps
to maintain a safe and stable balance with natural
infrastructure that can promote environmental health
and diversity.
The $1,000,000 set aside for implementation of plan
recommendations would create approximately 7.1
construction jobs, potentially creating employment for
local and regional contractors in addition to 8.4 new
support jobs from increased materials and equipment
sales for suppliers and support industries.

Benefits from a decrease in road closures include
reduction in lost trips to local restaurants and shopping
areas, where many residents go to socialize.

Project Cost-Benefit Analysis
This project closely relates to the Additional Hydrologic

Completion of this project supports two of

Given the limited number of routes
throughout the Town, a single bridge or

the difference between life and death
for a resident.

significantly outweigh the costs of this project.

Community Rating
System Participation
Project Background
FEMA promotes the benefits of the NFIP Community
Rating System (CRS) as a voluntary program that
recognizes and rewards communities for floodplain
management activities that exceed minimum NFIP
requirements through the receipt of discounted
insurance rates. FEMA initiated the CRS program 1990
and currently has over 1,200 participating communities.
As of August 31, 2014, the Town of Blooming Grove had
91 NFIP policies in-force and $103,376 in total annual
premiums, with an average annual premium of $1,136.
All communities participating in the NFIP CRS begin at
a Class 10 rating, which offers no insurance discount.
Lower class numbers (i.e., Class 1 to Class 9) are reached
based on the number of community achievements in
the six core flood loss reduction areas important to the
NFIP. These areas include the following:

Image is courtesy of
http://www.passaiccountynj.org/index.aspx?NID=1041

1. Reduce liabilities to the NFIP Fund;

Description of Project

2. Improve disaster resiliency and sustainability of
communities;

This project would fund professional services to
develop an application for the Town of Blooming Grove,
potentially in partnership with the Village of South
Blooming Grove, to participate in the NFIP CRS and
reduce flood insurance rates for Town residents. The
Town would seek to achieve a Class 8 rating, which
would result in a 10% discount in flood insurance policy
premiums for policy holders in good standing.

3. Integrate a “whole community” approach to
addressing emergency management;
4. Promote natural and beneficial functions of
floodplains;
5. Increase understanding of risk; and
6. Strengthen adoption and enforcement of disasterresistant building codes.

Connection to the Disaster
In 2011, after the events of Hurricane Irene and Tropical
Storm Lee, Town residents submitted a total of 43 claims
(27.6%), the largest number of claims submitted in any
one year during the Town’s history of NFIP participation.

would also contain the following aspects:
• Educate the current Town Supervisor, Board
members, and the public about the CRS program
and its benefits; and
• Assist residents with resiliency-focused
residential property improvements and assist in
providing access to and awareness of needed
funding resources.

Cost Estimate
The total project cost is estimated at $60,000, which

The CRS program seeks to reduce risk to communities
by encouraging community flood mitigation and
prevention activities through the incentive of increased
insurance payment discounts.
One way in which the CRS program seeks to lessen risk
of floods to a community is by requiring communities
with Repetitive Loss (RL) properties (i.e., properties
for which two or more flood insurance claims of more
than $1,000 have been paid by the NFIP within any 10year period since 1978) to implement annual outreach
projects to the properties in the mapped RL areas that
have insurable buildings.
The CRS program also encourages communities to
complete more than just outreach to reduce their
repetitive flooding problems. Some of the other
activities that CRS encourages, although does not
require, including the following:
• Flood Protection Assistance: Technical assistance
on property protection.
• Floodplain Management Planning: Conducting an
analysis of the RL areas and identifying mitigation
strategies for these areas.

Diagram courtesy of https://suite.io/david-a-todd/2ejr2j6

Project Benefits
FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
Because the CRS program encourages the use of flood
mitigation, preparedness, and reduction activities
to achieve credits, it ultimately decreases flood
vulnerability and increases public safety by offering
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

• Acquisition and Relocation: Acquiring or
otherwise removing RL properties. Larger bonuses
are applied for Severe Repetitive Loss (SRL)
Properties (i.e., family properties that have had
four or more claims of more than $5,000 or two
to three claims that cumulatively exceed the
building’s value).
• Flood Protection: Retrofitting or otherwise
protecting RL properties. Larger bonuses are
applied for SRL properties.

through flood mitigation activities, empirical data

The CRS program gives communities multiple incentives

benefit of participation in the CRS program is the
discount in flood insurance payments that property

ENVIRONMENTAL
While this project anticipates few direct environmental
benefits, the CRS program provides a significant
opportunity for indirect benefits. CRS offers special
credit for activities that protect and/or restore natural
floodplain functions due to the benefits of maintaining a
floodplain in its natural state.
The CRS program encourages state, local, and private
activities that preserve or restore the natural state of
floodplains and ensure that they continue to perform
these necessary functions. The CRS program also
encourages communities to coordinate their flood
loss reduction programs with other public and private
activities that preserve and protect natural and
beneficial floodplain functions.

from disasters cannot flourish without the provision of
safe, reliable, and rapid emergency services.

Project Benefits
FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
Having pre-disaster plans in place will reduce local
emergency service response times, define roles and
responsibilities in advance to create ownership for
potential problems, and create more robust capabilities
via volunteer networks and elimination of redundancy.
The development of pre-disaster plans will enhance
the efforts of the Plan Area and the Village of
Washingtonville to successfully rescue, evacuate,
shelter, and perform other emergency services functions
necessary to directly protect the life and safety of Town
and Village residents.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
This project takes an all-hazards planning approach
to coordinate response efforts across all disciplines in
the Town of Blooming Grove, as well as the Villages
of South Blooming Grove and Washingtonville, during
times of disaster.
This project reduces risk in the following areas:
• The CEMP will identify specific prevention and
mitigation efforts of the Town of Blooming Grove
131

and the Village of South Blooming Grove. These
efforts will describe the activities taken to prevent
or minimize the impact of hazards in the Plan Area.
• The multi-hazard approach to the CEMP, along
with its multijurisdictional operational and
hazard specific annexes, will improve overall
response effectiveness to the Town of Blooming
Grove and its neighboring jurisdictions. In the
planning process, stakeholders will incorporate
knowledge of a full-range of hazards into disaster
and risk management. The CEMP will not only
review natural hazards but will also factor in
political strategies, technical analysis, operational
capabilities, and public understanding. The CEMP
update will also incorporate nationwide best
practices and lessons learned. This approach will
ultimately lead to greater effectiveness and costefficiency for emergency response.
• The update and development of plans, policies,
and procedures will involve, to a certain
degree, public participation. The involvement
of community members in the design and
implementation of plans helps to tailor the
plans to the vulnerabilities and needs of the
affected people.
• The development of volunteer organizations,
programs, and public-private partnerships will
increase the response capabilities within Blooming
Grove and its neighboring jurisdictions. These
organizations can offer government agencies
opportunities to combine resources and expertise
to act jointly to reduce risks and potential losses.

ECONOMIC
This project anticipates few direct economic benefits
(i.e., permanent jobs secured or added; potential
increase in economic activity); however, it offers a great
potential in increasing integration and synchronization
of emergency response efforts across multiple
jurisdictions. As a result, the Plan Area and the Village
of Washingtonville will see more efficient and effective
emergency response efforts, thus decreasing the
number of resources utilized and money spent during a
given year.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Project Background
Residents in the area of the Tappan Subdivision along
Barnes Road have experienced economic hardships due
to damage from multiple flood events. Because
of this concern, the Committee has investigated multiple
mitigation measures to try to reduce the impact of
flooding, including the potential creation of additional
flood storage along Satterly Creek to relocate floodwaters
away from the vulnerable, residential structures.
Unfortunately, preliminary hydraulic findings have
indicated that Satterly Creek already accesses a
significantly large floodplain, which means that it will
be difficult to identify additional storage areas for
local floodwaters. Additionally, the land surrounding

Satterly Creek has a very shallow grade, further
limiting flood storage potential due to the amount of
excavation required and the potential to run into a high
groundwater table.

Connection to the Disaster
Historic flood damages and losses in the area of the
Tappan Subdivision along Barnes Road have led to great
economic hardships for residents, as evidenced by the
number of FEMA NFIP—participating homeowners
along Barnes Road that are listed as RL/SRL plan
holders. Five of the nine RL/SRL properties in the Tow n
of Blooming Grove are located on Barnes Road. Barnes
Road properties received nearly $631,000 in NFIP
reimbursement due to Hurricane Irene and Tropical
Storm Lee.

This image depicts the location of Barnes Road levee project. Location
map courtesy of SCAPE / Landscape Architecture PLLC.

SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

134

FEATURED PROJECTS

Barnes Road Levee

FEATURED PROJECTS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Barnes Road and North Drive during Hurricane Irene Flooding. Photo
courtesy of Lorraine Tomassi.

Description of Project
This project proposes to address the recurrent flood
damage through the creation of a berm/levee located
behind the residences on Barnes Road, from 144
Barnes Road to the Satterly Creek side of the Tappan
Waste Water Treatment Plant. This berm/levee would
direct floodwaters away from homes toward the nonresidential side of Satterly Creek. This project would
also build on the base analysis from the NYRCR planning
process and would include a comprehensive analysis of
the Satterly Creek in the area of Barnes Road to evaluate
the quantitative potential flood reduction benefits of a
levee in this area.

Floodwaters cover the entrance to the Tappan Hill Water and
Waste Water Treatment Plant during Hurricane Irene. Photo Courtesy
of Rachel Robertson.

The initial hydraulic analysis using the existing HEC-RAS
model for Satterly Creek resulted in a 0.5-mile long
levee, approximately 7-feet in height (to an elevation
of approximately 340 feet), as necessary to mitigate
floodplain flows from impacting residences and the
wastewater treatment plant along Barnes Road. The
levee must be constructed to provide protection from
flood flows while limiting the impact on the WSE and
stream power.

Project Benefits
FLOOD PROTECTION AND
EMERGENCY RESPONSE
The Barnes Road Levee will have the most significant
impact on the residents of Barnes Road. This would
directly benefit a minimum of 16 residences on Barnes
Rd, although it would also indirectly benefit many
SECTION 4: PROPOSED AND FEATURED PROJECT PROFILES

of the nearby property owners by creating increased
access to local roadways during severe storms and
flooding events.
Benefits to the local community include: (1) improving
overall floodplain management and reducing the
frequency of flood damage to homes, businesses and
residents; and (2) ensuring redundancy in transportation
and access to roadways by decreasing the number
of road closures in a flood event (i.e., thus increasing
access to local homes, businesses, and other essential
facilities such as hospitals, doctor’s offices, etc.).
Additionally, this project will help benefit residents
and emergency response or other essential personnel
needing access during storm events. This is important
for emergency and essential personnel during events
136

FEATURED PROJECTS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

that result in evacuations, the need for distribution of
supplies, and any other need for emergency assistance.
Socially Vulnerable Populations may be at greater
risk during such events, and would also benefit from
securing the roadways. These residents may also have
more difficulty in leaving their homes, particularly
during storm or flood events.

ANTICIPATED REDUCTION OF RISK
HEC-RAS modeling shows that the proposed Barnes
Road levee can be placed in a manner that does not
significantly increase the WSE and stream power but
does remove the residences and wastewater treatment
plant along Barnes Road from the existing floodplain.

ECONOMIC
This project would create approximately 8.5
construction jobs, potentially creating employment
for local and regional contractors in addition to 10.1
new support jobs from increased materials and
equipment sales for suppliers and support industries.
This injected funding into the regional construction
supply line encourages economic growth through
additional induced spending. Induced spending occurs
as employees and businesses benefitting from the
construction work in turn spend money on other goods
and services. The potential induced benefit includes
additional permanent jobs, increased taxes, and
increased expendable income that may be spent on
additional local goods and services.
In addition to job creation, levees have been found to
have a variety of economic benefits. Data suggests that
total productivity and personal income are greater and
that poverty is less in counties with levees. Specifically,
the average county with levees produces nearly 3.3
times (or $2.6 billion) more in annual goods and
services, the average resident in a county with levees
earns $1,500 more per year, and the poverty rate
averages 2% less in counties with levees.
Although this data is general in nature as it has been
averaged across all counties in the U.S., it provides
a general snapshot of the potential secondary
economic benefits that the Town of Blooming Grove
137

could experience by maintaining safe and responsible
development of its floodplain, such as through the
construction of a levee on Barnes Road.
Additionally, the economic impact of the Barnes Road
levee will have significant implications for residents on
Barnes Road besides that of future development and
indirect secondary benefits. Many of the Barnes Road
residents have experienced economic hardship based
on the frequency of
flood events and total damage from floods impacting
their homes.
Depending on the amount of flood risk reduction, the
16 residences with NFIP policies may also experience
a decrease in their premium rates. Installing a levee
to reduce this flood damage will provide a significant
financial relief to these residents and give them an
opportunity to invest their increased savings in personal
expenses, long-term savings, and more discretionary
spending, such as eating at restaurants, attending
movies, and supporting local community events.

ENVIRONMENTAL
Hydraulic modeling shows that the proposed levee
should not significantly impact WSE or velocities in
the local vicinity or downstream portions of Satterly
Creek. This project does not anticipate any negative
direct environmental impact. Eliminating houses and
the wastewater treatment plant from the Satterly
Creek floodplain could greatly reduce the possibility of
household cleaners, petroleum products, untreated
wastewater, or other pollutants from entering the
stream and impacting the downstream community.

SOCIAL
A decrease in the number of road closures due to
flooding will serve as one of the most immediate and
notable effects. Secondary benefits from a decrease
in road closures will help prevent lost trips to local
restaurants and shopping areas, where many residents
conduct social engagements.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

• Strategy 1: Reduce the impact of riverine

Project Cost-Benefit Analysis
The NFIP reimbursed homeowners along Barnes Road
approximately $631,000 from flood insurance payments
due to Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm Lee in 2011.
This was just reimbursements; actual damages were
likely higher. The value of damages grows as time
passes, meaning similar flood events will cause higher
values of loss as time goes on.
Although levee construction has an expensive up-front
cost, the levee will justify its existence after only three
flood events, at a maximum, similar in scope to those
of 2011.

The Barnes Road levee will have significant implications for residents
on Barnes Road. Photo is courtesy of Eric Thayer.

residents and the expected decrease to their flood
insurance premiums.

This project can be implemented in one year.

REGULATORY REQUIREMENTS
To be certified, the levee must comply with the
requirements of Chapter 44 of the Code of Federal
Regulations (44 CFR), Section 65.2 and 65.10. The
project may also have to comply with Article 15
Protection of Waters and Article 24 Freshwater
Wetlands of the NYCRR.

JURISDICTION
This project will be under the jurisdiction of the
Town of Blooming Grove.

Barnes Road Levee
• Investment: $1,198,623
• Flood level reduction: The levee would completely protect homes along Barnes Road from riverine flooding.
• Assets protected: Immediate: Housing and Infrastructure Systems; Long-term: Potential benefit to other
assets (health and social services and economic).
• Repetitive flood properties removed: Although no RL/SRL sites will be physically removed, this project may
lead to properties losing the RL/SRL designation due to flood reduction and decreased flood damage in
future flooding events.
• Potential future loss prevented: Unknown, although Barnes Road properties received nearly $631,000 in
NFIP reimbursement due to Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm Lee.
• Jobs created: 8.5 Construction; 10.1 Permanent*

New MultiPurpose Shelter

Operations Facility

The Town does not currently have a reliable,
designated emergency shelter with sufficient
capacity to meet the Community’s needs during
major flood events. During Hurricane Irene, three
of the Town’s firehouses flooded. Two schools
also flooded, while the other three schools were
not accessible by all residents of the Town due to

Grove is at a premium. This project will identify a

responders, i.e., it would provide parking for their
vehicles. Town services, such as the senior center,

could relocate to this facility as well.

Town of Blooming
Grove MultiModal Emergency
Exit Trail Map

The Town of Blooming Grove currently hosts miles of
recreational walking and hiking trails, some of which
connect to regionally and nationally recognized
trail systems, although many residents and most
visitors to the Plan Area remain uninformed of their

modes of transportation in appropriate disaster

dissemination of a map of existing local walking and
hiking trails, as well as local pedestrian amenities,
such as local roadways with sidewalks, which may
be designed and distributed in combination with a
pedestrian evacuation plan.

Y

Barnes Road Levee

This project proposes to create a berm/levee behind the residences on Barnes Road, from 144 Barnes Road to the Satterly Creek side of Tappan Sewer
Plant, to deflect and keep the floodwaters away from the homes. Instead, the structural project would direct floodwaters to the non-residential side of
Satterly Creek. This project would build on and be informed by the base analysis from the NYRCR planning process to provide additional information and
recommendations for further actions to reduce flood impacts. This would involve a comprehensive hydraulic analysis of the Satterly Creek in the area of
Barnes Road to evaluate the potential flood damage reduction benefits of constructing a levee.

Community Rating
System Participation

This project would fund professional services to develop an application for the Town of Blooming Grove, potentially in partnership with the Village of
South Blooming Grove, to join the NFIP CRS to reduce flood insurance rates in the Town. The project would aim to enter participating municipalities
into the program at a CRS Rating of 8, which would immediately translate into a 10% discount in flood insurance policy premiums for policy holders in
good standing. In addition to gaining membership to the CRS program, this contract would educate the present Town Supervisor, Board Members, and
the public about the CRS program and its associated benefits; and assist residents with resiliency-focused residential property improvements and with
access and awareness to needed funding resources.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Collaborative
Communications:
Public Engagement
For A Public Process
The term “collaborative communications” best describes
how the Committee, supported by the Consultant Team,
worked to engage the community in the development
of the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan. Committee
had an eye from the start toward implementing
solutions to increase recovery and resiliency. They
were keenly aware of the results of several devastating
storms, whose impacts were corroborated by the
numerous hazards, land use, and flood-related studies
conducted to date.

With a sentiment that recognized, “We’re
in this together,” the Committee decisively
moved to include the Village of South
Blooming Grove in the NYRCR Plan Area.
While this seemed like a logical move—because both
the Town of Blooming Grove and the Village of South
Blooming Grove share the same watershed and service
boundaries—this measure of collaboration speaks to
the readiness that residents expressed to turn findings
into actions. Moreover, residents and business owners
alike increasingly acknowledged that disaster events
do not discriminate, and that the best solutions and
remedies are mutually tackled.

Community-Driven,
Community-Targeted
The public engagement process that factored into the
development of the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR
Plan and resulted in the community-driven resiliency
initiatives was predicated on an understanding of which
local messaging channels would be most effective.
Formulated at the start of the planning process,
Committee Members actively engaged in strategizing
everything, from the messages to the methods by which

147

SECTION 5: ADDITIONAL MATERIALS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

gathered information and encouraged attendance at the
four Public Engagement Events. These events were held
at strategic points during the planning process, both
to cull public opinion and to inform the public about
the qualitative and quantitative findings made at key
benchmarks in the planning process.

Regular Committee Meetings

Public Engagement Events were ideal forums for the community to
learn about what causes flooding, how it can be mitigated, and what
might be required to achieve greater resiliency. Photo is courtesy of
Tetra Tech, Inc.

they would reach those who live, work, and service the
Town of Blooming Grove.
From the outset, a spirit of inclusiveness underscored
the approach to public engagement.

Residents from very distinct residential
segments in the NYRCR Plan Area comprised
both the Committee and the regular group
of attendees that attended the biweekly
Committee Meetings.
This cross-section included the frequently flooded,
single-family, residential Tappan Subdivision
neighborhood; the private lake communities of
Tomahawk Lake (in the Town of Blooming Grove) and
Merriewold Lake (in the Village of South Blooming
Grove); and the privately owned, socially vulnerable
Mountain Lodge Park community. Additionally,
representatives from regional watershed groups, the
Village of Washingtonville, and the Orange County
Planning Office contributed to Committee meetings and
messaging efforts.
Outreach to residents, homeowners, non-resident
property owners, business owners, and community and
social service organizations across both the public and
private sectors served as the local blueprint for public
engagement and education. In particular, this outreach

SECTION 5: ADDITIONAL MATERIALS

The Committee, composed of local residents,
businesspeople, and community organizational
stakeholders, has met every other week since the
Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Program inception
in June 2014 to discuss issues, areas of critical need,
and prospective projects. The meetings also served
as a forum to advance the vetting and outreach
processes. Guided by formal meeting agendas and
Robert’s Rules of Order, each meeting included time
for public comment in an open, inviting environment,
with meeting notes recorded. Once reviewed by the
Committee, meeting notes were made available to the
public on the NYRCR Program website. Meetings were
held at the Town of Blooming Grove Senior Center or
Town Hall, both located on Horton Road in the Town
of Blooming Grove.
Public presentations by representatives from the Town
of Blooming Grove, elected officials from the Village of
South Blooming Grove, volunteer emergency services
personnel, and others complemented the regular
Committee Meetings.
This level of participatory collaboration sought two
ends: (1) to capitalize on existing flood mitigation
efforts; and (2) to maximize available resources for the
planning process and project implementation.
The NYRCR Program website, along with many local,
community, and civic organizations’ websites, advertised
all Committee meetings and Public Engagement Events.
Committee Members were directly involved in the
public engagement campaign.

148

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Targeting the Message and Methods
The means and methods by which people send and
receive messages serve as a direct reflection of their
geographic locations and demographic characteristics.
Where and how people live provides a predictable
gauge to understanding how they acquire and share
information about community happenings.

Information Gathering and Information
Sharing at the Grassroots Level
Within the first few weeks of the planning process,
Committee Members distributed surveys to neighbors
and local emergency response personnel as a way to
gauge interest, gather ideas, and engage them in the

A collaborative mapping exercise conducted at one of the Public
Engagement Events enabled residents and business owners to offer
personal accounts of their experiences during and following Hurricane
Irene. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech, Inc.

NYRCR planning process. Committee Members and
local municipal representatives disseminated fliers and
surveys about the NYRCR Program throughout their
respective neighborhoods and via e-mail.
These surveys, available in hard-copy and electronic
format, compiled public opinions about community
needs and opportunities relative to storm recovery,
mitigation, and resiliency.

149

The Committee also shared surveys at Public
Engagement Events. These surveys enabled participants
to rank the importance of the six recovery support
functions (RSFs), as defined initially by FEMA and
adopted by the NYRCR Program. The surveys included
support of vulnerable populations as a category also.
While not an official RSF, this factor plays a significant
role in modern emergency management capabilities.
Public input about these needs and opportunities,
in light of existing and outstanding community
resources, critical assets, and essential redundancies,
became instrumental to the development of Proposed
Project, Featured Projects, and Additional Resiliency
Recommendations.
Top concerns noted by survey respondents included
impassable roads, damage to utilities, inability to access
essential services, and damage to infrastructure. Other
concerns ranking high among responses focused on
the length of utility service interruption, structural
damage to residences, inadequate storm warning and
information about recovery service access, damage to
natural/cultural resources, damage to septic tanks, and
untimely rescue. These survey results were gathered
between July and October 2014.
The Committee also worked with Community officials
and leaders of various service organizations to obtain
input on critical community needs during and following
disasters. Particular inquiry was made to agencies
providing services to vulnerable populations who would
have difficulty with self-evacuation, sheltering-in-place,
and post-event recovery activities. The inquiry included
obtaining survey results from emergency services,
highway department personnel, and fire and police
representatives across the Town Blooming Grove
NYRCR Plan Area.
The Consultant Team also conducted telephone and
in-person interviews with key community stakeholders
who did not participate in the online survey.
These interviews enabled more in-depth discussions
that yielded valuable input relative to prospective
needs and project opportunities.

FIGURE 5.1 – VOTING RESULTS

The public provided valuable input on damages from major storms.

Opinions gathered from the online survey were
consistent with other data and opinions collected,
concurring that infrastructure should be the primary
focus area for NYRCR Program funding.

Interactive Exercise and Public Opinion
The first Public Engagement Event included an interactive
“voting” exercise. During this event, attendees identified
funding allotment priorities by RSF categorization.

Public Engagement Events drew interest in Blooming Grove. Photo is
courtesy of Tetra Tech.

Figure 5.1 provides a visual verification of the results
from these exercises, which mirror both findings
gathered by surveys and anecdotal evidence gathered
throughout the planning process. The Community
identified its primary priority as Infrastructure, with
Community Planning and Capacity Building ranking
second. Housing and Vulnerable Populations also scored
fairly high on Community concerns.
Attendees of the first and second Public Engagement
Events shared their personal experiences with flood
and storm damage, particularly concerning the events
of Hurricane Irene and Tropical Storm Lee. Plan Area
overview maps served as the canvas for participants to
provide accounts, circle areas of roadway damage or
inhibited access, and generally record their personal
experiences with storm damage.

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Getting the Word Out
The Consultant Team’s demographic profile for the
Plan Area showed that the target population for the
residential outreach campaign included a significant
number of residents belonging to a hard-to-reach
demographic, i.e., aging populations that either do not
have or do not regularly use the Internet. Committee
Members became very proactive and determined to
reach residents in all neighborhoods of the Plan Area.
Committee Members distributed posters and placards
about the NYRCR Program and what benefits it offered
individuals living in the NYRCR Plan Area.

Public outreach efforts incorporated the following
modes of multi-media messaging:
• Media alerts;
• E-mail blasts;
• Posters and placards;
• Lawn signs;
• Newspaper advertisements (print/online);
• NYRCR Program, Town, and community
organizational websites;
• Personal social media; and
• Frequently Asked Questions (FAQ) flyers.

Committee Members were particularly proactive in
public engagement and served as the sounding board
for message design and delivery.

The public provided valuable input on damages from major storms. Photo is courtesy of Tetra Tech.

SECTION 5: ADDITIONAL MATERIALS

Town of Blooming Grove NY Rising Community Reconstruction Plan

Public Engagement Events
Four community-wide Public Engagement Events were
conducted between July 2014 and January 2015. These
Public Engagement Events had the following end goals:
• Provide education about the NYRCR Program (all
Public Engagement Events);
• Obtain input from residents, property owners, and
business owners (all Public Engagement Events);
• Review the draft sections of the NYRCR Plan (Public
Engagement Events 2 and 3); and
• Review recommended projects based on public
input and scientific analysis (Public Engagement
Event 4).
At all Public Engagement Events, participants were
encouraged to review the NYRCR Program and Plan
materials, including maps of targeted and prospective
project areas.
As a direct result of an increasing groundswell of
information available about the NYRCR Program and
the Town of Blooming Grove NYRCR Plan, along with
the active Committee leadership, attendance grew with
each successive Public Engagement Event.

Risk Assessment Methodology
The following section outlines the initial risk assessment
for assets in the Plan Area. The analysis incorporated
the baseline methodology, enhanced by specific
assumptions at the request of the Committee. The
baseline methodology included four major components
of the analysis: Risk Area, Hazard Factor, Exposure Score,
and Vulnerability Score.

data includes:

(ESRI) (2010);
• Federal Communications Commission (2012);
• Federal Communications Commission (FCC);
• Insurance Services Office, Inc.;
• National Oceanic and Atmospheric
Administration (NOAA);
• National Park Service (2011);
• National Pipeline Mapping System (2003);

Community Value
The Committee worked with the Consultant Team
to assign community values for the identified assets.
Assigning a community value allowed the Committee to
get a geographic picture of where important community
assets were located and allowed the Committee to
weigh potential project impacts on the Town in an
informed manner. It did not factor into the risk score of
individual assets.

Description of Methodology
The risk assessment for assets in the Town incorporated
NYRCR baseline methodology, enhanced by specific
assumptions at the request of the Committee. The
baseline methodology included four major components
of the analysis: Risk Area, Hazard Factor, Exposure Score,
and Vulnerability Score.
The hazard score of 3 was assigned for the hazard factor
in the tool (100-year flood water level occurring within a
100-year planning time frame).
The exposure score was determined by the sum of a
base score (derived from the risk area in which the asset
is located) plus 0.5 point for each of the six landscape
feature conditions described in Table 5.3, if present.
A base score was assigned for exposure to each asset,
depending on highest-class risk area (Extreme = 2, High
= 1, and Moderate = 0.5) in which a significant portion
of the asset is located.

TABLE 5.3 – LANDSCAPE ATTRIBUTE VULNERABILITY ASSUMPTIONS
Feature Type

Feature Definition

Defensive Flood Protection Measures

These measures are absent, below base flood elevation (BFE), in
poor condition, or lack maintenance commitment.

Elevation

The asset site is below the Base Flood Elevation (BFE).

Freeboard

Elevation of the habitable or occupied portion of the asset is less
than 2 feet above BFE.

Point of Confluence

Asset is within an area subject to increased flood risk (based on
Consultant Team’s judgment or Committee guidance) because of
a confluence of merging streams.

Stormwater Discharge

Asset is within an area subject to increased flood risk (based on
Consultant Team’s judgment or Committee guidance) because of
stormwater system discharge.

Vegetated Streambank Buffers

Asset is within the floodway fringe (based on FEMA definition).

Low-lying lands, such as those used for farming, can experience significant wash-outs and runoff during major storm events.
